# Causes of Animal Colouration.
## I. Preliminary Experiments on Extracts.

By

### Hans Przibram and Leonore Brecher.

(From the Biological Experimental Institute of the Imperial Academy of Sciences in Vienna [Zoological Department]¹.)

*(Received on 23 July 1918.)*

*Archiv für Entwicklungsmechanik der Organismen*, vol. 45 (1919).

> **Full translation.** A complete English rendering of Przibram & Brecher's study of the causes of animal colouration — the tyrosinase/dopa pigment-forming system — with the tables and figure legends.

> ¹ An abstract of this work appeared under the same title as Communication No. 31 from the Biol. Experimental Institute of the Imperial Acad. of Sci., Zoolog. Dept., Director H. Przibram, in the Acad. Sitzungsanzeiger [Proceedings Notices] No. 17, 1918.

## I. Introduction.

By "animal colour-dress [colouration]" is understood the entire colouration of an animal, the colouration that is carried by it on display. It is thus to comprise the brightness, hue and patterning of the superficial body-layer and of the lower layers possibly shimmering through it, but not the colourations of inner organs or fluids that are invisible from outside. Even though in many cases a significance in the physiological respect may attach to these latter colours — for example the redness of the vertebrate blood being closely bound up with the respiratory function — nonetheless the colour-dress is of still greater significance for the weal and woe of the animals.

Above all it is the protective colouration — which offers its bearer the possibility of remaining unnoticed by enemies, or of creeping up unnoticed upon prey — that has set before our eyes the biological importance of particular colour-dresses. In the Darwinian and Darwinist expositions concerning the efficacy of natural selection through the survival of the fittest, we therefore encounter again and again examples from this domain. The endeavour to attain, out of chance variations — or also mutations, unknown to us in their inner essence — an understanding of the development of the colour-dress could, no more than in other fields of development, lead to a deeper penetration into the causal structure; for it is precisely the elimination of the "chance element" that must be decisive for the construction of a biology on a par with the exact natural sciences.

With the denial of any direct connection between the acting external factors and the appearance of the animals, the extreme selectionists have moreover deprived themselves of the possibility of imitating, by the experimental path, the coordination of a particular colour-dress to particular conditions of illumination and to other climatic conditions. In most recent times the attempt has been made, in the sense of Lamarck, to trace the assumption of a protective colour-dress back to an active exertion of the animal — hence at least to construct an indirect connection between the external factor and the colour.

The influencing of the colour-change of many animals by light, which turns out differently after blinding, seems to speak in favour of a mediating role of the eye-impression; yet the operation of such an apparatus already presupposes the existence of the mechanism and the chemism.

Alongside these complicated relationships, however, there are also to be found, under the influence of the illumination, changeable colour-dresses whose [colour-change] proceeds independently of the eye-impression.

Above all it is the caterpillars of certain butterflies which, during pupation, are capable of copying the colour of the substratum up to a certain degree — and indeed, according to Poulton's experiments, even when the eyes cannot perceive the light. These simpler processes seemed to me the best suited to procure for us an insight into the true causes of animal colouration. When Frl. [Miss] L. Brecher therefore turned to me for guidance toward experiments on the colour-variability of animals, I proposed to her the butterfly pupae as suitable objects, especially since, from earlier investigations, points of reference for the chemisms coming into consideration were at my command.

Hitherto one had concerned oneself little with the chemical side of the question — one could almost say not at all. The few attempts to trace the agreement of the animal's colouration with the colours of its surroundings back to a simple colour-photographic process could from the outset have found application only to the physical colours, but not there where pigments colour the animal-dress.

Now, however, Frl. Dr. Brecher was able, in her dissertation on the pupal colourations of the cabbage white [*Pieris brassicae*], to demonstrate — in accordance with my expectations — that in the case of the butterfly pupae we are dealing with extractable pigments which colour the pupal skin partly localized in the form of chromatophores, partly in diffuse distribution. The different colouration of extracts from the various modifications brought about by colour-effects, as well as the discovery of reactions which allow several modifications to be distinguished in the test-tube, have also excluded the possibility that the matter could merely be one of contraction and expansion of chromatophores.

Since the statements of the earlier authors concerning the direct action of the coloured surroundings upon the colour-dress of the caterpillars becoming pupae had been confirmed by Brecher's work (as well as by the simultaneous and independent experiments of Dr. Dürken), and since in our experiments the specific effect of the colour, as against mere changes in the intensity of the total illumination, had been placed beyond question, the path on which we would arrive at the uncovering of the causal connection between coloured surroundings and animal colour-dress seemed to us clearly marked out.

There had to exist a direct influence of light of different wavelength upon the formation of the pigments decisive for the modifications of the pupal colouration, and it had to be possible to succeed in producing the same in the test-tube. The possibility of such processes had been set forth by Wiener (1895) in his treatise "Colour-photography through body-colours and mechanical colour-adaptation in Nature," but had not been proved.

The experiments carried out by Frl. Dr. Brecher together with me have, beyond expectation, led quickly to satisfying results, which leave but little room for Wiener's principle. While it shall be left to Dr. Brecher to bring forward the complete explanation of this colour-adaptation in the continuation of her work on the pupal colourations of the cabbage white, in the present first part of the work I wish to set out the general principle on the basis of the preliminary experiments carried out on the most diverse animal and plant extracts — [a principle] which will then permit us, in the second part, to trace protective colouration and many other hitherto enigmatic problems of biological colouration back to chemical reactions.

It is a matter of the sensitivity of ferments [enzymes] towards light of different wavelength (and other external factors) — which, as I learned subsequently from the literature, had already been discovered by Wolfgang Ostwald, but had not been employed for the explanation of colour-modifications.

Among the ferments [enzymes] employed by Wo. Ostwald there was already the tyrosinase too, whose general significance for the coming-about of dark pigments in the animal kingdom had been demonstrated by my highly esteemed friend and teacher Otto v. Fürth. Unfortunately it is precisely Ostwald's tyrosinase experiments that have never been published, as the same was so kind as to communicate to me, in response to my inquiry, through Frau Dr. Ostwald.

The tyrosinase first prepared by Bertrand from the wood-fungus Halimasch [honey fungus], *Agaricus melleus*, has the property of oxidizing tyrosine and other "chromogens," in the air, into dark pigments, the melanins.

The obtaining of the ferments [enzymes] was carried out in all our experiments by the path indicated by Fürth: preparation of an aqueous extract, half-saturation with ammonium sulphate by addition of an equal quantity of saturated ammonium sulphate solution, filtration after the settling of the protein, and dissolving of the filter-residue. Whereas Fürth, for this solution, had employed sodium carbonate, we have used various other aqueous solvents, mostly 0.05 % sodium hydroxide solution. Where not stated otherwise, this solution is therefore to be understood. In order to be able at least to designate the relative strength of tyrosinase solutions of the same provenance, we have mostly taken the saturated solution as strength 1. In the case of the pupal tyrosinases this came about through dissolving of the filter-residue in the same quantity of fluid as had existed before the addition of the ammonium sulphate solution. These solutions too appear to me to be saturated, so that the dilution of the same to ½, ¼ etc. by corresponding addition of fluid would correspond to an analogous degree of saturation.

## II. Tyrosinases.

### 1. The colour-sensitivity of the tyrosinases.

#### a) Experimental technique.

For the testing of the colour-sensitivity of the tyrosinase reaction, in our first experiments a row of test-tubes of 1 cm diameter, each charged with 1 cm³ of saturated tyrosine solution and one drop of tyrosinase, were exposed to differently coloured colours [light].

The exposure took place either in little boxes accessible to daylight at the top and lined inside with coloured matt paper, of 25 cm length, 10 cm width and 17 cm height, or in cardboard boxes of similar dimensions, which were covered at the top with coloured gelatine and a white glass plate; [these] were, in a dark-room, subjected to continuous illumination by a 200-candle Osram metal-filament lamp.

Later it turned out to be more advantageous to expose only the tyrosinase to the different influence of light, in order to obtain distinct results. All later experiments were therefore set up in such a way that the tyrosinases which had been subjected to a pre-irradiation with definite kinds of light were dropped into a row of tyrosine samples exposed to ordinary daylight. In this way one can compare the effect exerted on the tyrosinase by the pre-irradiation on the samples set up side by side in one and the same test-tube stand, without being hindered in the judging of the depth of colouration by the different colour of the surroundings, or even having first to take the samples out of the stands.

For the lining of the test-tubes there served, in the wooden boxes, wooden stands covered with coloured matt paper, which are capable of taking up 8 test-tubes each in two rows one above the other, so that the lower row can thus be exposed to a lesser light-intensity than the upper. In the cardboard boxes there served perforated pasteboard strips, which were laid upon cross-bars near the long walls and the opening of the box and could be charged with 11 test-tubes each, for the reception of the samples. Those series which, after pre-irradiation of the tyrosinase, came to be set up in daylight, were accommodated in uncovered wooden stands of the two-row form, whereby the illumination of the upper and lower rows was not regarded as different.

In each experiment several identically treated samples were if possible set up, in order to avoid chance effects [arising] in single test-tubes. Several times irregularities showed up within these identically treated samples. These "variations" we have, in the course of the investigation, learned to trace back to definite causes and then also for the most part to avoid. Such experimental errors are conditioned:

A. By inexact adherence to the stated test-tube dimensions, in that narrower test-tubes offer a smaller surface for the oxidation, hence the darkening that appears proceeds more slowly;

B. by remaining-behind drops of the distilled water used for the rinsing-out of the test-tubes, which, as we shall see, does not remain a matter of indifference for the appearing colour-tone (cf. 3, p. 109);

C. by use of dark tyrosinases, which were kept in broad flasks into whose interior the daylight had not been able to penetrate, so that there are present in the fluid ferment-particles partly struck by the light, partly exposed to continuous darkness (cf. chapter on ferment-susceptibility or chromogens);

D. by infection from deficient closure of the cotton-wool plug brought into use, or from deficient sterility of the fluids brought into use. These experimental errors play a certain role with use of tyrosinases of the vertebrates and with animal chromogens. With the tyrosine samples to which fungus-tyrosinases or tyrosinases of invertebrate animals are added, sterilization of the test-tubes proves superfluous, indeed even, for a reason to be mentioned later, a source of error (cf. II, 2c);

E. by co-deposition of the ferment on the test-tube wall in the case of an oblique introduction of the drop-pipette. This error can be avoided if the drop-pipette is lowered down to near the tyrosine surface;

F. by unequal size of the drop emerging out of one and the same pipette in consequence of carrying-along of air. In order to avoid such differences entirely, we have repeatedly set up parallel series with samples in which a certain number of cubic centimetres of tyrosine was charged with just as many (or an aliquot number of) tyrosinase drops, and the test-tubes were charged with 1 cm³ each only after thorough mixing of the fluid by repeated pouring-over. It turns out that this method was not able to eliminate the irregularities mentioned under C., and that with careful dropping-in of the tyrosinase into the test-tubes already previously divided up into 1 cm³ of tyrosine each, equally good results could be attained. On the other hand, it constitutes a decided disadvantage of the common mixing of all samples that the initial stages of the colour-change cannot be well observed, because this does not begin to spread out from a definite point as it does when a drop of ferment is poured into a quietly standing tyrosine sample;

H. by unequal position of the test-tubes relative to the light-source. Differences in the intensity were sought to be avoided by having the test-tubes to be treated alike take up the same position relative to the light-source. This was achieved by a radial arrangement of the colour-boxes around the direction of incidence of the light. In the first experiments with the two-row test-tube stands in the colour-lined wooden boxes, the intensity-difference in the upper and lower test-tube row made itself distinctly noticeable.

For the lining of the colour-boxes and test-tube stands there served the same coloured papers which had served, in the experiments with living pupae and other animals, for the covering of the containers. It was precisely a matter of importance to us to show that the surrounding-colour effective upon the living objects is likewise capable of influencing the process in the test-tube. With the gelatine colours too, the matter for us was at first not one of exactly analysed kinds of light and light-strengths, but rather of being able to compare incident with reflected light, continuous with periodic daylight. The testing of the influence of qualitatively and quantitatively exactly determined kinds of light by means of spectral experiments, the interposition of colour-solutions or colour-glasses, shall form only the content of a later publication. It suffices us, for these reasons, to designate the colours of the papers with the current names in the spectral sequence: lilac (light violet), blue, blue-green, yellow-green, yellow, post-yellow, orange, red, purple; further the little-saturated colours pearl-grey, (yellowish) grey, brown, and the toneless ones, white and black; in order also to test the influence of darkness, a black-lined box was provided with a cap of black paper. There were thus in all 15 wooden boxes present, by which mostly the same series was not set up in all colours at the same time.

The gelatines can be designated as violet, blue, blue-green, green, yellow, orange, red and colourless. The colourless gelatine served for

### b) Course of the experiments

All experiments were registered under continuous monitoring, as required, whenever changes occurred. The registration was done by entering the intensity- and colour-tones that appeared by means of pastel crayons into pre-drawn diagrams. There would be no point in reproducing these graphic records. Given the great number of experiments, this would only have a confusing effect. In order to be able to offer clear presentations of the course of the experiments, I first carried out a tabular arrangement of the experiments, which proceeded according to the following points of view: First all those experiments were grouped which relate to colour-sensitivity, then those which relate to temperature-sensitivity, and further those which relate to the reaction-sensitivity of the tyrosinase. Within each of these three groups the experiments were then ordered according to the species used (plant kinds, then animal kinds), from which the tyrosinase had been taken. The further ordering resulted from the sequence of the recorded experiments, which in general agrees with the chronological succession of the experiments. After the compilation had been carried out, all experiments received consecutive orientation-numbers, which fill the first column in Tables II, 1–3 and may be designated in the text as "Or.-Nr." [orientation no.]. The booklet- and page-numbers given in the third column of the original protocols have for the reader only this much significance, that they make it easier to follow the chronological connection of the experiments of the various groups.

In this section on the light-sensitivity of the tyrosinase, what interests us about the course of the experiments is, first of all, whether, depending on the irradiation applied, an earlier or later onset [Angehen] of samples otherwise of the same kind takes place. Four possibilities would indeed be given: α) no samples go on [react]; β) all samples go on simultaneously; γ) the samples go on, to be sure, non-simultaneously, but irregularly; δ) the samples in particular colours regularly go on earlier than in others.

α) In experiments in which no irradiated samples go on, the simultaneously set-up dark-sample will decide whether it is a matter of a uniform action of all kinds of rays or of an inactivity of the tyrosinase altogether. In the preliminary experiments¹) we observed only the latter alternative. The causes will come up for discussion later (II, 3c).

> ¹) Later experiments, which will be published in further publications, have also realized this alternative.

β) If all samples go on simultaneously, then the cause of this can naturally not lie in the inactivity of the tyrosinase, insofar as it cannot be a matter of a colour-change of the tyrosine solution proceeding independently of an enzyme (cf. III, 1c). Simultaneous onset was observed only sometimes, and then [only] when the tyrosine samples with non-pre-irradiated tyrosinase were directly exposed to the colour-conditions; we have therefore every reason to assume that in this case the rays had not had time to act upon the tyrosinase before this had already begun to colour the tyrosine.

γ) Irregular onset of the samples, such that after the colouring of one or several samples under a particular pre-irradiation, samples from another pre-irradiation went on first, indeed even that some samples unexpectedly failed entirely, has occurred repeatedly, but relatively seldom. With the uncovering of the sources of error explained under the experimental technique, this irregularity could be partly eliminated, partly made insignificant.

δ) Apart from these faulty experiments, most showed a more or less far-reaching temporal difference in the onset of the samples of various irradiation, with almost complete temporal agreement in the onset of the tyrosinases that had been equally irradiated in each experiment.

In order to grant an insight into these relations, I have drawn together a table (B) from the experiment-tables, in which the individual orientation-numbers are entered under that colour through whose irradiation the samples first went on. From this table there then arose, again through counting the experiments in each colour-column, the clear Table C, which indicates in how many experiments each of the irradiation-colours used first caused the samples to go on.

Into this table all experiments coming into consideration for this question have been taken up; these are the Or.-Nr. 1–39; 42; 45; 50–79; 198–211 of Table A, thus altogether 85 experiments.

A glance at the last row of Table C gives us the following information about the frequency of the first onset in a particular colour:

αα) All irradiation-experiments failed in 11 cases.

ββ) All samples went on simultaneously in 9 cases.

So altogether 20 experiments out of 85, thus less than a quarter, turned out entirely negative.

γγ) A disturbance in the determination of the colour that went on first occurred only twice, in that after the onset of one sample in one colour, other samples went on before the remaining samples in the first colour got their turn. The two cases are designated on the tables by means of a γ in parentheses: they concern once Post-yellow [Postgelb], once White.

δδ) Among the 63 regular experiment-results regarding the first onset, 19 cases fall to Yellow-green, thus almost ¹/₃! furthermore 15 to Blue, almost ¹/₄!; then 6 to Green (gelatine), 4 each to Red and Purple, 3 each to Yellow, Dark, Grey, Black; 2 to Post-yellow, 1 to Violet (gelatine); Blue-green, Lilac, Orange, Pearl-grey and Brown, which last two were, to be sure, drawn upon only in few experiments, come away entirely empty-handed, but also the almost always likewise set-up White is — if we disregard the irregular case mentioned under γγ) — nowhere in first place; the same holds for control-samples, which were often made with non-pretreated tyrosinase simply set up in diffuse periodic daylight, without a reflecting container. In the experiment-tables these samples are given as "periodically" illuminated.

Although 18 different kinds of irradiation were used, in over half of our 63 regular experiments the samples go on first from Yellow-green or Blue. A chance occurrence appears excluded, since in each experiment usually 2 to 4 samples from each irradiation used for the pretreatment had been set up.

In reality the relation in favour of these two colours is a still much better one than it appears at first glance. In the tables the colour of the gelatine was designated as "green," which lets through somewhat fewer yellow rays than would correspond to the paper designated "yellow-green." If we disregard this trifling difference and unite the experiments that went on first in "green" with those that went on first in "yellow-green" — which were indeed never set up simultaneously in these two colours — then we obtain 25 times a first onset in the yellowish green.

We further note that in the experiments Or.-Nr. 1 (Agaricus) and Nr. 60 (Sphodromantis), in which the Yellow went on first, no Yellow-green or Green had been set up; in the irregularly-onsetting Or.-Nr. 29 (Agaricus) both Yellow-greens followed upon the one sample that went on in White; in the experiments Or.-Nr. 200 and 205, those in Yellow-green followed upon the samples that went on in "Post-yellow."

If we count all experiments in which predominantly yellow or yellowgreen rays caused the earliest onset, then we obtain 30 cases, almost half of all positive experiments.

If we combine in analogous manner the experiments that went on first in blue or red-blue rays, then we obtain 24 cases, by reckoning in the gelatine-colour rubricated under "red," which indeed also lets through rays of the blue end, although to our eye it appears only red.

There remain 3 experiments each that went on first in Black, Grey and Dark. With respect to the Black it is to be mentioned that in Or.-Nr. 210 (Agaricus, NaCl) Blue and Yellow-green had not been set up; in Or.-Nr. 68 and 70 Yellow-green was likewise not set up, whereby in Nr. 68 Blue went on at once after Black, [and] in Nr. 70 alongside Blue almost all colours went on at once as well.

In those experiments in reflected daylight, in which Blue went on first and Black had also been set up, namely Or.-Nr. 26, 31, 51 (Agaricus); Or.-Nr. 67, 69 (Pieris); Black followed in second place (once in third place after Lilac, Or.-Nr. 77, Cuvia); upon Purple in first place, Black likewise followed in second place in Or.-Nr. 78 and 79 (Mus).

In the experiments covered with gelatine and glass-plate, illuminated with a 200-candlepower Osram metal-filament lamp, in which, owing to the relatively low light-intensity, never such distinct differences appeared as in daylight, the coordinated position of the Black with the blue-containing rays does not appear.

Everything indicates that in the black reflection-box more blue, violet, possibly ultraviolet rays are active than in the white, grey or dark box, or in the pearl-grey and blue-green box which reflect Blue to a certain degree.

To cite the sequence of onset in detail would be too long-winded. The experimental material is set out in the tables. In general it can be said:

a) In reflected light (daylight illumination).

In experiments with earliest onset in Yellow-green, all other samples go on before those in Blue; first upon Yellow-green follow Post-yellow, Yellow and Dark; later follow the remaining non-red-containing colours, the red-containing ones, and the toneless ones.

In experiments with earliest onset in Blue, all other samples go on before those in Yellow-green; first upon Blue follow the toneless and red-containing ones; later the non-yellow-containing ones, then Dark, Yellow and Post-yellow.

In these two most numerous groups of experiments the sequence of the colours is thus quite reversed.

Only in a few experiments does Purple (and Post-yellow) go on before Blue and Yellow-green, the Yellow-green last (Or.-Nr. 198, Agaricus H₂O; 206 Agaricus H₂O₂).

In the experiments that went on first in Dark, there comes in second place Yellow-green (Or.-Nr. 12 Agaricus), Post-yellow (Or.-Nr. 14 Agaricus) or periodic (Nr. 63 Smerinthus).

b) In transmitted light (Osram illumination).

In experiments with earliest onset in Green, all go on before Red (Or.-Nr. 16 Agaricus, Orange still later); first upon Green follows Dark (Or.-Nr. 4–6 Agaricus) or, in the gelatine-experiments, the Black coming close to it, as explained above (Or.-Nr. 15 Agaricus); then Yellow or Orange (or White respectively).

In experiments with earliest onset in Red, all go on before Dark and Green (Or.-Nr. 8, 9, 10, 18); the sequence of the remaining colours, given the small differences in the onset-time, cannot be determined with certainty. The same way behave the experiments that went on first in Violet (Or.-Nr. 54 Dixippus) and Blue (Or.-Nr. 11, 17 Agaricus).

c) Disregarding the kind of illumination, we can bring the rules set up under a) and b) into a simpler form.

Complementary colours (yellow–blue; green–red) exert upon one and the same tyrosinase, under otherwise equal conditions, an opposing influence, in that we regard as the mean onset-time the one without colour-influence, [and] in that the one colour of a complementary pair accelerates the onset, the other delays it.

To the green-yellow rays stands closest the influence of darkness, whereas to the red-blue [rays stands closest] that of White and Black. The light mixed from other combinations of non-complementary main colours, thus blue-green, yellow-red [light], further the strongly unsaturated brown and toneless grey colours, have hardly any other influence than the periodic daylight reflected from no near surface.

Before I appraise these experiment-results in a critical investigation, a few facts about the course of the experiments shall still be adduced, which are of importance for the judgement.

With regard to the mentioned slightness of the temporal colouration-differences in the gelatine-experiments, weight must be laid on the fact that in the daylight-experiments, working with stronger light-intensities, the difference in the onset-time can be a quite considerable one.

In favourable cases the onset-times extend over weeks, indeed months, and repeatedly experiments could be registered in which it did not come at all to the colouration of all samples before the experiment-series, owing to complete evaporation of the tyrosine-samples or of other circumstances not consisting in a premature breaking-off, came to its end.

By filling such samples into very small little glasses and good corking, they can serve for a long time for demonstrations, such as we presented to the Zoological-Botanical Society in Vienna on 11 April 1917¹).

> ¹) These differences remained in existence for a further year, until the complete evaporation of many of these samples. (Addendum May 1918.)

At the end of such experiments, then, either the tyrosine-samples charged with Yellow-green-irradiated tyrosinase are clear as water and all others deep black, or, conversely, those irradiated with Blue are clear as water and the rest deep black. In the course of the experiments, however, there are also found times in which, e.g., Yellow-green, Post-yellow and Dark appear clear as water, all others deeply coloured, or conversely. Sample-series corked at the suitable moment and thereby withdrawn from further oxidation also let the irradiation applied be recognized by the differing intensity of the colouration: alongside the extremely favoured or inhibited colourations there appear half-dark, wine-yellow to red-brown tones in those samples whose tyrosinase-drops had been irradiated by non-preferred colours, such as Blue-green, Orange, Brown, Grey.

The graduated succession of colour-tones which are run through from the first onset up to the definitive colouration of the tyrosine or of other chromogens will only be returned to in a later section (on the reaction-sensitivity of the tyrosinases), whereby the causal connections of the appearing variations will come up for discussion.

I will here turn to the question which probably every reader will want to pose after the expositions so far: Under what circumstances does the preference for Yellow-green set in, under what [circumstances] that for Blue? The summary treatment of all the individual experiments is unable to give us any information about this. Let us first try to decide, with the aid of Table C, whether the origin of the tyrosinase from the various species is decisive; we then find with Agaricus Yellow-green as accelerator in 16 experiments (predominantly yellow- and green-containing irradiation in 26), Blue in 11 (predominantly blue- and red-containing irradiation in 14) experiments; with the animals the corresponding experiment-numbers would be: Yellow-green 3 (Yellow and Green 5); Blue 4 (Blue and Red 9). A restriction of the tyrosinase going on first from Yellow-green to particular species thus does not take place, just as little [a restriction] of the blue-onsetting [tyrosinase]. With the Halimasch [honey fungus, Armillaria] there go on, in about ⅔ of the positive experiments, the tyrosinases pretreated with yellow-green rays first, [while] with the animals the blue rays are more often preferred, and the number of the negative experiments is proportionally greater than with the plant object, namely with the experiments carried out on animals more numerous than with the fungus, which was used twice as often. The significance of these relations I shall likewise return to under the reaction-sensitivity.

If the species is not decisive for the effectiveness of a particular irradiation, then a quantity of tyrosinase prepared by us at one time could still yield always the same results with respect to the colour that goes on first. Apart from the mentioned irregularities as consequences of experiment-errors, this conjecture holds, insofar as we set up our experiments simultaneously.

If, on the other hand, we have established the first-onsetting ray-colour for a tyrosinase, say by charging samples with freshly-prepared tyrosinase in the colour-boxes and leaving [them] therein until the onset, then this colour does not by any means hold for future experiments which we carry out with tyrosinase-samples that we took from the same tyrosinase, but had exposed to the coloured influences for differing lengths of time.

After the action of the particular irradiations, there sets in a change in the first onset of samples later charged with the pre-irradiated tyrosinases, which leads to a complete reversal of the first-onsetting colours, so that then in place of Green, Red, in place of Yellow-green, Blue, and in place of Blue again Yellow-green can appear. A few examples of this "reversal" [Umkehr] are clearly presented in Table D. The pre-irradiation-time needed for the reversal is a quite fluctuating one; it will require a more extensive and more uniform experimental material in order to establish its values quantitatively.

Finally the effectiveness of all pretreated tyrosinases dies out, just as that of non-pretreated [ones]; about these time-relations too we can for the present only say that some tyrosinase-portions kept themselves capable of action for months, while others failed very quickly.

Whether the age of the tyrosinase, i.e. the time elapsed from its preparation up to the setting-up of the experiment, plays a great role for its influencing by colours, I am unable to state; the 28-day-old Agaricus-tyrosinase of the experiments Or.-Nr. 15–19 still gave distinct results and reversal, whereas others failed completely already after the first setting-up of the experiment, following shortly upon the preparation. If once-influenced tyrosinase is, before its instillation into tyrosine-samples, exposed for some time to ordinary periodic daylight, then the pre-irradiation can, in later set-up samples, nevertheless still come distinctly to expression. Thus in the experiment Or.-Nr. 36, with the charging of the tyrosine-samples ten days after a seven-week irradiation, in the experiment Or.-Nr. 50 with setting-up 17 days after the same. It is in this experiment-series especially interesting that in experiment Nr. 50 the Yellow-green has still fully preserved its precedence in onset at a time when in experiment Nr. 49, with continuous pre-irradiation of the same tyrosinase, no difference between Yellow-green and Purple is present, since the continued pre-irradiation leads precisely to the reversal, as then experiment Nr. 52 of the same series, with first onset of the blue-irradiated tyrosinase, shows.

The reversal takes place, as is to be gathered from Table D, with very different strengths of the tyrosinase; with Agaricus it was established from ¹/₁₀ to ¹/₄₀ dilution of one and the same tyrosinase-preparation. It sets in both with the constant transmitted illumination of the gelatine-experiments (e.g. Or.-Nr. 15–19), and with the colour-boxes set up in reflected daylight, where during the night darkness prevailed.

As with plants (Agaricus), the reversal comes about in analogous manner with animal tyrosinase (Smerinthus, Or.-Nr. 62–65). The reversal can take place at least twice (Agaricus, Or.-Nr. 24 to 51). From the examples selected for Table D, which are meant to illustrate the reversal, it might seem as though initially the tyrosinase, under yellow-green irradiation, would always go on first, and [as though] the earlier onset in Blue were always to be traced back to a reversal. But that is not the case; with Pieris (Table A, Or.-Nr. 67 and 68) we find Blue and Black going on first right from the start. With Agaricus-tyrosinases in a solution other than the usually used soda-concentration, the reversal from Yellow-green to Blue appears much more quickly. This reference to the influence of the reaction-state points again to the reaction-sensitivity of the tyrosinases, and in this chapter the explanation for the foregoing differences of the onset and of the reversal will be given.

### c) Experiment-results

The course of the experiments leaves no doubt that in the little boxes lined with colour or covered with colour-gelatines a significant influence is exerted upon the tyrosinase. Before we, however, trace this influence back to the colour of the irradiation, we must investigate whether, along with the colour, other factors have not been changed in our experiments, which were able to produce differences.

Since the light-intensity of the various colours used will be a different one, it is first of all to be taken into consideration whether we are The reversal occurs, as may be seen from Table D, at very different strengths of the tyrosinase; in *Agaricus* it was established from ¹⁄₁₀ to ¹⁄₄₀ dilution of one and the same tyrosinase preparation.

It occurs both with the constant transmitted illumination of the gelatine experiments (e.g. Or.-No. 15–19) and in the colour-boxes set up in reflected daylight, where darkness prevailed during the night.

As in plants (*Agaricus*), the reversal comes about in an analogous way in animal tyrosinase (*Smerinthus*, Or.-No. 62–65).

The reversal can occur at least twice (*Agaricus*, Or.-No. 24 to 51). From the examples selected for Table D, which are meant to illustrate the reversal, it might seem as though at first the tyrosinase always reacted [went off] first under yellow-green irradiation, and that the earlier reaction under blue was always to be traced back to a reversal. But this is not so; in *Pieris* (Table A, Or.-No. 67 and 68) we find blue and black reacting first right from the start.

In *Agaricus* tyrosinases in a solution other than the customarily used soda concentration, the reversal from yellow-green to blue sets in much more quickly. This indication of the influence of the reaction state again points to a reaction-sensitivity of the tyrosinases, and in this chapter the explanation for the foregoing differences in the reaction [going-off] and in the reversal will be given.

#### c) Experimental results.

The course of the experiment leaves no doubt that, in the boxes lined with colour or covered with coloured gelatines, a significant influence is exerted upon the tyrosinase.

Before, however, we trace this influence back to the colour of the irradiation, we must investigate whether, along with the colour, other factors were not altered in our experiments which could have produced differences.

Since the light-intensity of the various colours used will be a different one, it must first be considered whether we are not dealing with intensity-differences of the light alone, while the kind of ray or wavelength has no immediate bearing.

It is well known that an immediate comparison of the light-intensities of different colours is extremely difficult. It further depends on whether, by intensity, we wish to draw upon the degree of strength of the light exerted upon our eye, or a more objective effect, brought about for instance by the blackening of photographic papers. Neither of the two methods gives us a correct picture of the acting energy-magnitude: on the one hand [our eye is the more sensitive measure] for the quantities of energy arriving by way of the yellow-green rays, the photographic substance for those of the blue-violet spectral ends.

In our colour-covered boxes, which receive light mixed with white, the violet — produced by laying a blue and a red gelatine one over the other — appears the darkest; there follow blue, blue-green, orange, red, green, while yellow appears to us the lightest. Measured by means of photographic paper, red and orange prove the least effective; there follow yellow, violet, green, blue-green and blue.

In figures, the intensity calculated from the blackening of the photographic paper amounted, in the gelatine experiments, [as follows]: in the dark-box, red and orange 0, black 1, yellow 2, violet 4, green, blue-green and blue 5, white 6.

Had we now wished to abide by the photographic intensity, then dark, red and orange should always have given the same result, and on the other hand green, blue-green and blue, which does not at all correspond to the course of the experiment. If we let the brightness appearing to our eye count as a measure for the light-intensity, then violet with dark would have to form the one extreme, yellow with white the other, [and] red and green would not produce great differences — which again would contradict the facts of the experiments.

We have indeed seen that the extremes standing opposed to one another in their effects were green and dark on the one hand, red and blue on the other.

If we turn from the gelatine experiments to the photographic boxes, these present more complicated conditions in so far as, in addition to the coloured light, there is always also mixed white light present.

Photographic measurement gave the following figures:

| Dark | Red | Purple | Orange | Blue-green | Blue | Lilac | Black | White |
|------|-----|--------|--------|------------|------|-------|-------|-------|
| 0 | 0—1 | 1 | 2 | 2 | 2 | 2—3 | 2—3 | 4 |

| Grey | Pearl-grey | Yellow | Yellow-green | Post-yellow | Green |
|------|------------|--------|--------------|-------------|-------|
| 4—5 | 5 | 6 | 6—7 | 7—8 | 8 | To our eye black appears as the darkest, white as the lightest, and nearest to this, yellow, yellow-green, post-yellow, grey and lilac as the next. Again the assignment of similar sensitivities would stand in complete contradiction to the observed assignment-groups of tyrosinase-sensitivity: according to the photographic intensity, violet, blue-green and blue would have to act alike, [and] dark with yellow, yellow-green and post-yellow would form the extreme; according to the visual impression, white and black would in addition have to form the greatest opposites; in the experiments, however, we always met with the coordinated groups yellow-green, post-yellow and dark on the one side, blue, black and white on the other side.

If, after these contradictions, it is already impossible to trace the action of the colours exclusively back to the strength of the light, there may yet still be pointed out various circumstances which had made such a conception improbable from the outset.

As the smallest light-intensity we must in any case regard darkness; now this, however, does not stand in its effect at one end of the row, but is attached after yellow-green and post-yellow and forms the transition to the little-effective middle groups of the blue-green, toneless and red-containing colours. Were the general light-intensity alone to be decisive, then in no case could the smallest action belong to the smallest intensity, [or] the greatest to the greatest: the greatest action would much rather have to belong to a determinable middle intensity, and since blue stands out the strongest in its action, our blue (and blue-containing red) would have had to possess just this intensity.

Then, however, with an absolute increase of the light-intensity in consequence of the use of an essentially stronger light-source, the descending branch of this intensity-curve would soon be reached and the difference between blue and yellow-green would be effaced. But just the opposite is the case: the essentially stronger daylight¹ lets the differences between blue and yellow-green stand out far more strongly than with the 300-candle Osram light, whose far smaller luminosity in all the colours, despite a similar duration, was distinct in the photographic comparison.

> ¹ Later experiments in the strongest daylight, however, did bring about such an effacement [of the differences] as against darkness.

Through the intensity of the daylight the differences were often greatly intensified, whereas in the dark-room experiments only relatively insignificant temporal differences in the colouring distinguished the colour-effects from the dark experiment.

The intensity of the pre-irradiation thus plays a great role for the distinctness of the phenomenon, but is not of decisive significance for the differences themselves.

There remains for us, then, only the conclusion that it is in fact the wavelength of the light that is responsible for the outcome.

At the beginning of our experiments, when I still believed that the action of the colours took place directly upon the melanin-formation, it astonished me greatly not to find the extremes of the various actions at the ends of the spectrum, as the analogue of the photographic processes would require. This wonder was fully cleared up by the perception that the action of the colours takes place upon the tyrosinase, but not upon the melanin-forming process itself. The colour-sensitivity of the ferments [enzymes] indeed does not, according to the statements of other researchers as well, follow the photochemical conversion rules of other light-sensitive substances.

In what manner, then, does light of different wavelength now act upon our tyrosinases?

From the section on the course of the experiment there follows the necessity, in assigning particular actions to particular wavelengths, of also taking into account the duration of the action and the reaction [pH] of the solution.

I will start first from the influence of the individual spectral regions and colour-mixtures upon the tyrosinase, most used by us and prepared in soda solution, of the honey fungus [Hallimasch] (*Agaricus melleus*), and from the direct setting-up of the samples in the coloured surroundings, that is, from the minimal pre-irradiation, which can last only from the charging of the tyrosine samples until their reaction [going-off]. It may be added here that the once-started sample, on removal from the original colour-condition and even on being brought into the opposite colour-surroundings, seems no longer to undergo any change in the course of the colouring, as a few preliminary experiments had shown.

The tyrosinase of *Agaricus* in 0.05 % caustic soda, pre-irradiated for the shortest time in yellowish green, yellow-green, yellow and post-yellow, promotes the melanin-formation more strongly than that placed in the dark: the tyrosine samples become intensely black in a short time, the duration depending on the strength of the tyrosinase.

The shortest pre-irradiation with blue inhibits the working-capacity of the same tyrosinase: the tyrosine samples for a long time do not react, or else remain altogether water-clear without melanin-formation. Somewhat less constant, but acting similarly inhibitingly, were black, lilac and white; in white-free light also the blue-containing red.

More weakly inhibiting acted grey, blue-green and the colours of the red spectral end; they acted almost always inhibitingly as against the colours of the yellow-green spectral region.

The spectral ends evidently do not form the ends of a series of action: rather, the maximum of melanin-promotion lies in the middle green-yellow region known to our eye as the brightness-maximum, while the maximum of melanin-inhibition can lie in the blue, but also in the blue-containing red.

The presence of yellow and green rays must be the promoting moment, the presence of blue and violet rays the inhibiting one; the red rays in themselves seem to occasion only a very slight inhibition.

With simultaneous presence of green-yellow and (red-)blue rays, their effects will work against one another; thus in various ways the same result can be obtained:

1. through mixture of blue and green rays = blue-green;
2. through mixture of yellow and green rays = orange;
3. through mixture of blue and yellow rays = grey;
4. through mixture of red and green rays = brown.

In all of these combinations it indeed never came to a complete inhibition of the tyrosine-colouration, but as against darkness a strong retardation of the reaction [going-off] was noticeable, which points to a preponderating influence of the blue and red rays as against the yellow and green ones. This clears up for us the experimental result in the two further cases:

5. through simultaneous reduction of the intensity of all rays we obtain the one acting similarly to blue: black;
6. through simultaneous presence of all rays we obtain the one likewise coming closer to blue: white.

Small differences in the mutual proportion of the rays in the mixed light can already produce diversities, if thereby a strongly effective colour can give the decisive turn: thus pearl-grey, as a grey with a bluish cast, acted decidedly more inhibitingly than a neutral (more yellowish) grey.

The natural action of the surrounding-colours upon the soda-fixed [?] *Agaricus*-tyrosinase therefore rests decisively upon the preponderance of one of the two effective colour-regions over the other.

Quite the analogous result follows for the later reversal of the promotion or, respectively, the inhibition with longer-lasting pre-irradiation. Then, with predominantly blue rays, the melanin-formation is promoted, with predominantly yellow-green ones the melanin-formation is inhibited, as against darkness. Admittedly the inhibition here sometimes set in fully in the darkness as well, so that then no further difference between their samples and the yellow-green ones could be established. The efficacy of the tyrosinase has in these cases simply already become extinguished in the darkness.

Similarly lie the conditions with the animal tyrosinases in soda solution. Only there often appears the phenomenon that from the outset the reverse action is present, i.e. blue reacts [goes off], yellow-green fails.

The same [holds] for *Agaricus* in alkaline solutions, so that we shall obtain the elucidation in the discussion of the reaction-sensitivity.

Before I pass on to this chapter, which is now to bring [us] closer to the chemical processes themselves, an objection must still be rejected which could be raised against the responsibility of the chemical action of the light rays of different wavelength for the discussed phenomena. Since, as is well known, the light rays of different wavelength differ also in their warming-action, the thought would from the outset not be to be dismissed, that only the temperature-differences bound up with the various irradiation had determined the outcome of our experiments. It was therefore necessary to study the influence which various temperatures are able to call forth upon the course of the experiments, namely upon the alteration of the tyrosinase with respect to its efficacy; to take into account the order of magnitude of the sensitivity of the tyrosinase to temperature; and to establish thermostatically any possible temperature-differences in the irradiation-boxes.

These investigations, however, then no longer relate to the light-sensitivity, but to the temperature-sensitivity, to which a next chapter may be devoted.

### 2. The temperature-sensitivity of the tyrosinases.

#### a) Experimental technique.

For the testing of the temperature-sensitivity, either the tyrosine samples charged with tyrosinase were exposed to different degrees of warmth at constant [level], or portions of tyrosinase were gradually warmed in a water-bath up to the degrees to be tested. Drops of tyrosinase brought directly out of the desired temperature were then dropped into empty test-tubes and cooled down to room temperature. Mostly the tyrosine was filled into these little test-dishes only the next morning, so that there could be no question of a direct action of the warmth taken along from the water-bath upon the melanin-formation. For the reading-off of the temperature attained in the water-bath there served a normal mercury thermometer, which was immersed in the water together with the little tyrosinase glasses and was, on a stand, screwed fast. The water-bath itself consisted of a cooking-glass [beaker] of Jena glass, filled with distilled water. The little glasses hung in the holes of a round cardboard disc covering the cooking-glass at the top, which was also bored through for the reception of the thermometer. As a heat-source there served a Bunsen burner placed under the water-bath, which rested on a tripod, clay triangle and wire gauze.

For the set-ups in longer-lasting, approximately constant temperature there served an electric thermostat (System Ehmann, from Rohrbeck-Vienna), which had been held at 37—39° C. For control there served the likewise approximately constant room temperature, 20° lower, such as used to prevail in the room where the thermostat was set up, namely in the box serving it as a base (17—19° C).

For the purpose of measuring the room temperatures in rooms, thermostats and irradiation-boxes there served mercury thermometers from O. Schulze (Berlin), compared with a normal thermometer, which had proved their worth with our constant-temperature chambers. The temperature-chambers unfortunately could not, owing to the war conditions, be kept in operation, so that the quantitative carrying-out of the temperature experiments had to be postponed to a later time. In order nevertheless to be able to set up samples under comparable circumstances with approximately constant but slight temperature-differences, we undertook modifications of the irradiation arrangements.

For the reception of the test-tubes [Eprouvetten] holding the tyrosinase and tyrosine samples there served larger test-tubes, lined along the half of their circumference lengthwise with the corresponding coloured papers. For darkness, an entirely black lining and a cap of black paper were used. Into one such larger test-tube, 2—3 smaller test-tubes can be sunk one above the other. A series of larger test-tubes provided with the photographic colour-conditions was exposed in a test-tube stand to the room temperature, while a second, identically charged stand in a glass trough covered with little glass plates represented a higher [temperature], one [standing] in a water-container with through-flow a lower temperature. The differences between the freely exposed series and each of the others were no greater than 2° C; in all, the temperature-differences must, on account of the length of the experimental duration and on account of the failure of the water-cooling in the warmest hours of the day, on average have been still smaller.

Still smaller diversities showed themselves under differently coloured — white, blue, red-yellow, green-yellow — glass covers, of which one consisted of transparent glass, while three so-called "Sennebier bell-jars" [were filled] with a solution of copper vitriol [copper sulphate] or, respectively, potassium bichromate or picric acid. Under a sheet-metal dark-cover, too, no appreciable temperature-difference showed itself. Under these covers were placed wooden stands with two holes, of which the one served for the reception of the thermometer, the other for the reception of one of the larger test-tubes described above, but without coloured covering.

#### b) Course of the experiments.

The experiments with great temperature-differences took, according to the application of the degrees of warmth to the various components of the melanin-formation, a different course.

1. Pre-warming of the tyrosinase can, as Fräulein Dr. Brecher first found for *Pieris*, have a colour-change of the incipient tyrosine samples as a consequence. The temperature-degree required for this is the one which we are to take from Table E, which contains the optima of the pigment-formation with statement of the "onset-colours" (»Angehfarben«) for the various tyrosinases; and in [the region] where still traces of tyrosine-discolouration occur, in the optimum and maximum, lie the temperatures at which the weakened tyrosinase-action can bring about an essential alteration of the colour-tone, mostly around 50° C; above 70° hardly any ferment-activity can be recognized any more.

2. Pre-warming of the tyrosine has no influence on the course of the melanin-formation.

3. Pre-warming of the tyrosinase and of the tyrosine has the same influence as if only the tyrosinase had been pre-warmed.

4. Brief warming of the tyrosine sample already mixed with tyrosinase has an effect analogous to pre-warming of the tyrosinase, namely the reversal towards the more violet tones, that is, the formation of the pigment in red when higher temperatures than 40° are applied.

5. Constant warming of the tyrosine samples already mixed with tyrosinase produced, even with tyrosinases which had failed at normal temperatures, at least a rose-colouration. This experimental course made it necessary to test the influence of constant temperature-elevation.

6. Constant warming of the tyrosine to about 40° without addition of tyrosinase coloured this likewise only slowly, after a few days, red-violet, after that always rose-red. This experimental course proved itself, with longer duration at ordinary temperature in aqueous solution, also with the tyrosine samples located there.

7. Old tyrosine solutions prepared years ago, which had not yet come into contact with tyrosinase, showed a red to red-violet colouration, without having been exposed to a higher than the normal room temperature¹).

Tyrosine samples of 1 cm² took on, in six to eight weeks at room temperatures of 15—20° C, a weakly rose colour, which deepened slowly later.

8. Pre-warming of the tyrosinase between 35 and 55° brought about, in an experiment with *Mus decumanus* (Or.-Nr. 102), that the tyrosine samples held at 38° coloured less than with the tyrosinase warmed above 55—60°.

Small temperature-differences below the optimum of the tyrosinase-action had neither on the tyrosine nor on the tyrosinase any noticeable influence on the onset-speed or onset-colour. At the ordinary temperatures fluctuating between 15 and 25° C, an influencing of the tyrosinases located in the colour-boxes by the temperature-differences of the colour-lining was therefore little probable.

The measurement of the temperatures simultaneously prevailing in the colour-boxes at the place of the little tyrosinase-glasses yielded complete agreement. Insofar as [the temperatures] in the reflection-boxes themselves [differed] from the dark-box, [it was] by less than ½°, and the slight deviations below ½° were not constant for particular boxes, hence without significance for the explanation of their definite effects. Still less can the temperatures in the gelatine-covered boxes have played a decisive role, since, given the lesser intensity of the outcome, far smaller temperature-differences would have had to secure perceptible deflections.

In the series mentioned occasionally with the experimental technique — in water-through-flow, glass-pane warming and free set-up — there were according to all the data only weak differences between the series to be perceived. Unfortunately, in consequence of the weak tyrosinase-action, no differences according to the colours were to be perceived.

Among our colour-impressions the colour, with smaller temperature-difference, clearly showed the influence of the colour: the arrangement of the increasing red-colouration was: white, blue, greenish-yellow, dark, post-yellow.

#### c) Experimental results.

The temperature-sensitivity of the tyrosinases at ordinary room temperatures is evidently not great. Only with pre-warming to 40° do the differences in the course of the experiment become clear, namely the differences ranging between the two extremes "white-pale" and

> ¹) The participation of fission-fungi was, however, here not excluded.

"ink-blackness" (Tintenschwärze) that are to be attained through various irradiation.

It is now certain that this effect of the rays does not rest upon temperature-differences, but is to be attributed to their various chemical effect. One could nevertheless object that the pre-warming had not been compared with a longer irradiation, in so far as one might thereupon know that the more intensively irradiated samples reacted [went off], although the effectiveness of the various rays also must only begin at moments later (e.g. Or.-Nr. 4 ff.). This also confirms the unstoppability of a once-initiated colouring process.

On the other hand it is clear that higher temperature accelerates the oxidation of the tyrosine, hence that with essentially warmer temperatures the tyrosine samples charged with [the same] tyrosinase should colour more rapidly than those at lower temperatures. Thereby the difference between the rays could no longer remain clearly maintained, since the lesser warmth-component of the relevant colour-surroundings would be overlaid by the artificially produced great temperature-difference (cf. e.g. Or.-Nr. 77—79).

That this is nevertheless in no way the case, [is shown by the fact] that real temperature-differences, such as are produced through pre-warming of the tyrosinase, come to bear, [and] that the [samples] charged with [warmed tyrosinase] come on after 38° C. With weakly acting rays the experiments give, at lower temperature (up to 25° C), [the result] that the tyrosine samples without tyrosinase begin to colour, [whereas] the tyrosinase samples that coloured later did not [colour] over the tyrosine samples not mixed with ferment, [but] remained water-clear (e.g. Or.-Nr. 52).

The tyrosinase that has become colour-inhibited through irradiation showed not only this peculiarity within a longer interval, but it is even able to counteract the colour-promoting action of warmth upon the tyrosine-solution actively.

So difficult as it must also appear at the first glance to attain a satisfying result in the disentangling of the chemical relations, we have nevertheless had the luck to recognize the right guiding-thread, because [it] permitted us to proceed with these concrete chemical conceptions. It is the importance of the reaction-state for the colour-finding of the tyrosinases, which is influenced in various manner through irradiation.

We turn therefore now to the next chapter, of the reaction-sensitivity of the tyrosinases.

For the conclusion of this section it may be mentioned that the inhibiting of a process through a temperature-inactivated ferment, which through far-reaching heating loses this capacity, in our case is not yet present. Cramer and Bearn (1906) found that the pepsin or rennin inactivated at 50—60° is still capable, effectively, of inhibiting non-heated pepsin or rennin solution, [whereas] pepsin or rennin heated to 100° added no inhibition any more.

According to the hypothesis of Armstrong and Bayliss (in Ostwald), the explanation would run as follows: The process consists of two stages: 1. the formation of a compound from substrate and enzyme; 2. the splitting of this compound.

At 60° the enzyme has retained the capacity for compound[-formation], but lost the capacity for later splitting; the process therefore proceeds no further to its end. The compound thereby also protects the substrate against other decay. At 100° the enzyme also loses the compound-capacity, so that the substrate is no longer subject to a splitting of the compound.

One could also think of an antioxydase, whose effectiveness is dissolved [destroyed] at 60°, so that the simultaneously present oxydase becomes effective only at higher temperature. Czapek (1903, 229) gives such an antioxydase, whose inhibition of the oxidation-processes ceases at 62°.

After discussion of the reaction-sensitivity of the tyrosinase we shall not be able to fend off the impression that the explanation of the phenomenon discussed above might also be traceable back to reaction-states of the same tyrosinase (or of the same enzyme).

### 3. The reaction-sensitivity of the tyrosinases.

#### a) Experimental technique.

About the experimental technique there is not much more to say than what has already been said in the analogous sections of the 1st and 2nd chapter.

The action of chemicals was carried out either through solution of the tyrosinase in the relevant solvent or through later addition of the same to the already otherwise dissolved tyrosinase, or finally through addition to the already incipient or already-reacted tyrosinase-tyrosine sample. Frequently here, for the purpose of simplifying the manipulations, graduated test-tubes were used.

The testing of the samples for acid and basic reaction took place by means of litmus paper, which however does not suffice for quantitative determinations. It is therefore planned, in future to make use of the new acid- and alkalimetric methods given by Traube (Traube and Somogyi, 1914), as well as, with the testing of colourless liquids, to draw upon colorimetric indicators and titration.

#### b) Course of the experiments.

When temperatures between optimum and maximum of the temperature-influence on tyrosinase produce red onset-colours in place of more violet ones, one could at first be tempted to refer the red, lighter colour to a lesser concentration, which had arisen through ineffectiveness of a part of the tyrosinase-particles. But this is already contradicted by the subsequent colour-change towards red with the use of non-pre-warmed tyrosinase, but with subsequent warming of the already incipient tyrosine sample.

If we intentionally alter the concentration of a tyrosinase, e.g. of the soda-dissolved *Agaricus*, in that we dilute the prepared solution to ½, ⅓, ¼ etc. through addition of the solvent (NaOH, 0.05%), then we find precisely with the high concentrations intensely red-brown, with the lower ones violet tones occurring, whereby no elevated temperatures are applied (Or.-Nr. 121: Konz. 2—¼; 181: ¼; 185: ⅛; 136—140: ¹⁄₁₀—¹⁄₁₆₀). It now looks again precisely the reverse, as if to the lower concentration a shifting of the rose onset-colour towards blue might correspond. Admittedly the first onset-colour is, even with the customary experimental arrangement, always rose, which again would stand in contradiction with the initially to-be-expected lesser concentration-effect of the not-yet-completely dissolved (or finely distributed) tyrosinase. The lesser the concentration, the later does the tyrosinase begin at all.

If we alter the experiment thus, that we drip one drop of tyrosinase each into 1, 2, 3 etc. cubic centimetres of tyrosine, then the brown colour increases likewise with the progressing dilution, which here is occasioned not through soda-solution but through aqueous tyrosine (Or.-Nr. 174). If, on the other hand, I take for each number of cubic centimetres an equal quantity of equally-concentrated drops, then the red-brown colour of the samples remains persisting, whereby indeed the concentration in no way was altered (Or.-Nr. 175). I can however subsequently alter the red colour to lilac, if I simply dilute with distilled water, hence dilute here through dilution of the reaction-state of the solution (or suspension) subsequently (Or.-Nr. 150).

All these apparent contradictions allow themselves to be unravelled in satisfying manner, if we take the reaction-state into account.

The *Agaricus*-tyrosinase dissolved in 0.05% NaOH possesses from the outset a basic reaction, which is already demonstrable by ordinary litmus paper (IV, 14).

If I reduce the basic reaction through the addition of saturated ammonium sulfate (Or.-Nr. 144, 149) to the tyrosinase-solution, then the onset-colour can rapidly change from rose to lilac. The reaction-change here occurring without essential concentration-change of the tyrosinase now evidently sets in also in the earlier mentioned experiments, where through dilution at the same time the basic reaction was, so to say, "diluted along with" it. The colour-shifting towards blue has therefore in these concentration-experiments too been linked with a reduction of the basic reaction. The absence of the blue-colouration with the use of an aliquot drop-number to the number of the tyrosine-quantities will also be able to be interpreted as a consequence of unaltered basicity.

On the other hand it can be shown, through the addition of 4 drops of 0.05% NaOH solution to a cubic centimetre of tyrosine, that the basic reaction of this solution increases with longer warming to 38° C, hence that the reddening of the samples at higher temperature can also be traced back to the increasing basic reaction. (Traube 1914, S. 316 was able to demonstrate by means of his new azidometric methods that blood-sera inactivated through warming to 56° increase in alkalinity.)

Just as the incipient ones [the samples in the process of onset], samples already gone over to rose can be made lilac through reduction of the basicity, addition of distilled water or common salt (Or.-Nr. 150, 151).

We have hitherto spoken of the onset-colours only with *Agaricus*-tyrosinases in 0.05% NaOH-solution. The question now arises how other tyrosinases behave. Tyrosinase obtained from a commercial mixture of not more closely determined fungi went off blue (Or.-Nr. 213); the red colour therefore does not lie in the plant provenance itself.

The animal tyrosinases in the same solution reacted, in so far as they reacted at all with stronger colour-formation, always [coming on] brown-violet to blue. The testing with litmus paper showed, in agreement with our hypothesis, that despite the basic solvent these tyrosinases were in a barely basic to non-basic state. In order to prove the correctness of this hypothesis further, on the one side, from basic *Agaricus*-tyrosinase, the residue after evaporation was taken up again in distilled water (Or.-Nr. 157, 158); on the other side, the likewise obtained residue of a *Saturnia*-tyrosinase (Or.-Nr. 231) was dissolved again in 0.5% NaOH-solution. Corresponding to the expectation, the tyrosine samples charged with the more strongly basic solution of the animal tyrosinase now took on first a rose and only slowly a more violet colour, whereas those charged with the less-basic-made tyrosinase of *Agaricus* soon showed iron-violet tones instead of rust-brown ones.

All these experiments suggested testing whether the first onset-colour too might be attributable exclusively to the momentary reaction-state of the sample, without regard to the earlier provenance of the tyrosinase.

For this purpose, solutions of *Agaricus* were prepared also in other solvents, and indeed with the concentrations given in Table F (d—k). It has however shown itself that the first onset-colour directs itself not after that [provenance] altered through the solvent, but after the original reaction-state of the tyrosinase. Also the tyrosinase dissolved in distilled water, common salt, ammonium sulfate, hydrogen peroxide, which in none of these solutions any longer allowed a basic reaction to be recognized, went off first rose or red, in order then admittedly to become violet much faster than in the 0.05% NaOH-solution and to strike ever bluer tones. Boric acid and acetic acid too did not let the *Agaricus*-tyrosinase begin decidedly with the blue colour characteristic of the animals. In spite of the small applied concentrations, these two acids already had an essentially lesser colouring-effect than the aforementioned solvents not provided with pronounced acid-character. The optimum of the acidification was thus already overstepped; even earlier the same is reached for hydrochloric-acid addition (Or.-Nr. 160), corresponding to the strength of this acid¹). *Agaricus*-tyrosinase dissolved in 0.5% NaOH yielded no colouration either (Or.-Nr. 159); the maximum of the basic effectiveness lies thus between 0.05 and 0.5% NaOH.

> ¹) When Fürth and Jerusalem (1907, S. 162) state that with animal tyrosinases every acid-addition had quite abolished the tyrosinase-action, this rests certainly upon the too-great quantity of supplied normal-acid, which amounted to 0.2 to 1 cm³ per 3 cm³ liquid. The "promoting" effect of the alkali here occurring I refer to the acid reaction of the animal tyrosinase, which had only been brought down to its optimum. This acid reaction emerges from the very rapid flocculation which the authors observed (S. 163). The butterfly-blood used for the preparation of their tyrosinase reacts according to Poulton (1885) acid, according to Geyer (1913), in contrast to Landois' statements, certainly not alkaline. We have also always found the tyrosinase prepared therefrom acid.

If we compare the rate at which the *Agaricus*-tyrosinase dissolved in 0.05% NaOH proceeds in relation to the other, not too strongly acidic solutions, these show themselves to be considerably in the lead. (Or.-Nr. 179–183; 184–188; 191–195; 198–202.) A decrease in basicity accelerates the tyrosinase action, as has already been stated by earlier authors (Gessard 1901; Fürth and Jerusalem 1907, p. 154) (cf. the preceding remark).

This acceleration of the tyrosinase action could also be the reason why, in the colour-development with acidic tyrosinase, the rose or red preliminary stage does not arise, but rather the violet or blue colour appears at once, which with weakly basic tyrosinase at ordinary temperature is also reached later.

Samples that have turned violet can, by the addition of bases, again take on a more reddish or yellowish tone; even almost black-coloured samples can, by appropriate dilution with basic agents, be discoloured brown-yellow, and with acidic ones blue-grey. Such examples are compiled in Table F, Column VI (*Agaricus*), VIII (*Dixippus*), IX (*Sphinx*), X (*Saturnia*), XII (*Pieris*), XIII and XIV (*Smerinthus*) (cf. also the unidentified fungi, Or.-Nr. 213–216).

The filtrate obtained after half-saturation with ammonium sulfate behaves similarly; it still contains the chromogen of the species in question and its tyrosinase, hence forms melanin (VII, *Agaricus*), and must behave analogously to a tyrosine sample with tyrosinase dissolved in ammonium sulfate (as XIII, *Smerinthus*).

We shall return, when discussing the chromogens, to the details of the course of the experiment upon the action of the aforementioned chemicals on the first filtrate of the half-saturated ammonium-sulfate–chromogen–tyrosinase solution. Here I should first only mention that, upon the addition of ammonium sulfate, a precipitation sets in with time, which leads to a settling-out of the dark melanin that has formed. This precipitation sets in the more rapidly the more strongly the solution has been acidified by the ammonium sulfate. In a much slower manner the same phenomenon also occurs in common-salt solution, more strongly in the strong sulfuric and hydrochloric acid (cf. the preceding remark).

If a tyrosinase that had been dissolved in ammonium sulfate has been exposed in portions to pre-irradiation by various colours, then the precipitation in the tyrosine samples occurs first in those portions which had been exposed to yellow-green, post-yellow and yellow, while it occurs last in the blue pre-irradiated ones (Or.-Nr. 202, 207, 212, *Agaricus*). The course of the experiment thus behaves as though the yellow-green rays had brought about an increase, and the blue ones a decrease, of the acidity-degree of one and the same ammonium-sulfate concentration.

The same impression of a relative acidification by the yellow-containing rays we also obtain from an experiment with soda-dissolved tyrosinase (Or.-Nr. 28), in which the order of onset on the first day did not follow regularly the otherwise usual arrangement of the pre-irradiation colours, since after yellow-green there followed not post-yellow and dark, but first one sample each of grey, purple, black, white and blue. On the next day, however, the samples then coming on from post-yellow and dark were nevertheless coloured lilac, like those from yellow-green, while all the others had taken on the red-brown colour. The differences in the temporal onset may have rested on unintended concentration-differences, such as different drop-size (cf. above, Experimental error II, 1 a), while nevertheless the acidity-degree may be influenced by the irradiation.

Besides the rose onset-colour corresponding to a basic reaction-state, and the lilac one belonging to an acidic reaction-state, there also occurs a green one. Its appearance seemed at first very sporadic, and so far we have not yet ascertained the exact conditions for being able to produce it at will at any time. It first occurred in extracts from fully worked-up *Dixippus* in the dark, then in mixtures of *Agaricus*- with warm-blooded-animal tyrosinases, but always only in a few samples, mostly alongside completely failing ones. With the use of very dilute *Smerinthus*- and finally also of just such *Agaricus*-tyrosinase, green onset-colours likewise came about. It thus seems that not mixing or impurity of the tyrosinase as such is to blame, but rather that, at a strength just barely sufficient for onset, the green colour comes about. Thus, with the very slowly proceeding basic tyrosinase (Or.-Nr. 193, *Agaricus*, 237, *Smerinthus*) a green sample appeared alongside lilac.

In pre-irradiation, yellow-green, post-yellow and dark seemed to favour the transient appearance of green (Or.-Nr. 49, 50, 51); likewise dissolving the tyrosinase in hydrogen peroxide (Or.-Nr. 206, 208). Blue pre-irradiation of this H₂O₂-tyrosinase produced, alongside green, also lilac.

Often a greenish colouration arises in the lowermost part of the test-tube in samples that were coloured blue-violet by animal tyrosinases in the upper or middle part (Or.-Nr. 232, *Smerinthus*, 255, *Pieris*); the same shows itself with the *Agaricus*-tyrosinase dissolved by hydrogen peroxide, which indeed comes on rose but quickly deviates to violet (Or.-Nr. 167, 168); but also with the use of basic honey-fungus (*Halimasch*) extracts (Or.-Nr. 165, 166) of strength 1. A slight shaking is able at once to destroy this stratification. Since shaking generally must favour the advance of the oxidation-process by supply of oxygen, its acceleration of the colour-deepening, with violet-colouration, may rest upon this. I think that the stratification of the greenish zone at the bottom of the test-tube could be traced back to the lack of oxygen, hence again to a minimal condition for colour-development at all.

There is a chemical agent which produces a yellowish-green colour both after the entry of the mixture of tyrosinase and tyrosine (Or.-Nr. 178–183, *Agaricus*) and also in the case of melanin-formation already further advanced (Filtrate I; Or.-Nr. 130); this is nitric acid (HNO₃, cf. Table F, k). If the tyrosinase dissolved in NaOH, 0.05%, is treated with nitric acid, the samples indeed come on rose, but a precipitation of orange-red substance soon ensues (Or.-Nr. 190; perhaps corresponding to the xanthomelanin of Fürth (1899)). The same arises also as a transitional product in the treatment of Filtrate I (Or.-Nr. 130). The fluid remains yellowish-green.

Since concentrated ammonia (NH₃, Table F, a), thus a strong base, likewise calls forth a similar precipitation with a following (here orange-red) solution of Filtrate I (Or.-Nr. 133), one would think not of a general acid- or base-action, but rather of a nitration in these processes.

Violet samples of *Pieris* (Or.-Nr. 251) gave with nitric acid likewise a yellow-orange precipitate, and with ammonia a "blonde" solution. Yet it must not be concealed that concentrated caustic soda brought about the same thing (Or.-Nr. 249), so that a nitration need not come about solely through direct insertion of the introduced nitrogen-group. The green colour, however, does not appear with the basic actions; for it these difficulties fall away.
