Although the fermentative origin of the yellow-green colour is certain, it is still an open question whether it is a matter of a reaction-state of the tyrosinase or rather of a second enzyme, which would be identical with the guaiac-bluing peroxidase. In this direction further experiments are being undertaken. This enzyme, which according to W. Ostwald's investigations behaves, with respect to its occurrence as well as to its origin in the living caterpillar-body, oppositely to catalase, could be held responsible for a greenish colouration, since extracts, according to the statements of the said author, are the more yellowish the less catalase, hence also the more peroxidase, they contain. If the peroxidase is really different from the tyrosinase, then with a low tyrosinase-content a yellowish-green colouration could come to appearance, which with stronger tyrosinase-action does not become visible owing to the red- to violet-colouration. Also in the precipitation of the "xanthomelanin" this concealed colouration could then appear again.

## c) Experimental results.

As the result of all the experiments just mentioned, there emerges the great sensitivity of the tyrosinase toward the reaction-state. This sensitivity already expresses itself in the differing onset-colour of tyrosinases of different provenance, in that the reaction-state of the original fluid, originating from the living body, plays the decisive role.

The basic *Agaricus*-tyrosinase comes on red, the acidic butterfly-pupa tyrosinase blue; in between lie amphoteric and neutral transitions. If tyrosinases are subsequently dissolved in another solvent, then the tyrosinase in the tyrosine sample indeed does not react at once with a change of onset-colour, yet it nevertheless proves sensitive, in that later the new stimulus is responded to. *Agaricus*-tyrosinase made acidic soon yields violet, butterfly-tyrosinase made basic yields rose tones.

The same change sets in when the reaction-state is correspondingly altered in tyrosinase already brought together with tyrosine; even the samples already come on can be promptly altered in their colouration by reversal of the reaction.

The preferential appearance of violet colouration with yellow-containing pre-irradiation and darkness, as against blue-containing pre-irradiation, as well as the earlier precipitation in ammonium-sulfate solution with yellow pre-irradiation, the later one with blue, permit the conclusion that the initial favouring of melanin-formation by yellow rays must rest upon an acidification of the tyrosinase brought about by these same rays, and the corresponding inhibition by blue rays upon an action of these in the opposite direction.

The reaction-state of the tyrosinase, altered by the light, uncovers the secret-means by which the wavelength of the pre-irradiating light influences samples that all seem set up under the same conditions, but come on quite differently.

We now grasp the relative ineffectiveness of the mixed light, in which the acidifying action of the yellow rays is opposed by the alkalizing action of the blue rays, so that a middle reaction-state comes about, similar as in the less effective blue-green and yellow-red rays.

But also the later reversal of effectiveness of pre-irradiated tyrosinases finds its explanation without strain: namely, even small additions of acid accelerate the destruction of enzymes, whereas alkaline reaction protects the enzymes somewhat, compared with neutral solutions (Fernbach, Tappeiner in Ostwald, p. 11). If, then, yellow irradiation acts acidifyingly on the tyrosinase, it will, with time, as the acidity-degree increases, destroy the tyrosinase, hence its effectiveness will be abolished and no melanin-formation will set in. If blue irradiation acts alkalizingly, the tyrosinase will remain capable of action longer. Since freshly prepared enzyme tends, after some time, to gain in effectiveness, as we too have observed, the preservation of effectiveness under longer blue irradiation can lead to an exercise of the effectiveness. The recovery of already inactivated enzymes is, too, no unknown phenomenon and can be responsible for the periodic alternation of states under constant irradiation. Yet in the experiments hitherto it has never come to a complete recovery of the blue-irradiated tyrosinases, unless from the outset their effectiveness was greater than that of the yellow-irradiated ones.

Such behaviour characterizes the acidic tyrosinases, especially the animal ones, and agrees very well with the acidification-hypothesis. Here the alkalizing action of the blue rays is at first not yet able to remove the acid-state and therewith the onset. On the other hand, the yellow pre-irradiation over-acidifies the already acidic tyrosinase and destroys it, or at least the just available active particles of it.

The over-acidification should also furnish the explanation for the failure of onset of basic tyrosinases under strong light-action of yellow, while yellow-green and post-yellow (still) came on. Examples of this are an experiment with nocturnal continuation of the illumination by Osram-light in the daylight-boxes (Or.-Nr. 3), experiments with 16–17-day daytime pre-irradiation (Or.-Nr. 12, 13). After one month's pre-irradiation, the effectiveness of the yellow-green too had been extinguished in the same tyrosinase (Or.-Nr. 14).

If the considerations advanced are correct, then, with serial use of the same tyrosinase after differing irradiation-duration, we must now come upon times at which the over-acidification by the yellow rays has rendered yellow, yellow-green and post-yellow ineffective, but blue rays still act inhibitingly, so that these two extremes occasion no colour-development, while the blue-green, yellow-red and tonelessly mixed colours make the samples come on. This prediction has repeatedly been confirmed in the experiments (Or.-Nr. 37, 38, 39, *Agaricus* in NaOH; 206, *Agaricus* in H₂O₂; 63, *Smerinthus*).

It would now, apart from the intended direct measurements of the acidity-degree of pre-irradiated tyrosinases, be interesting to know what kind of chemical group in the tyrosinase-molecule is subject to the acid-formation. Unfortunately the chemical composition of the enzymes is still entirely unknown to us, and we therefore cannot even give the normal formula for the tyrosinase.

Perhaps, however, precisely in the specific effectiveness of rays of different wavelength there lies a point of attack for drawing conclusions about the chemical structure of the enzyme-molecule? Since I must leave to later investigations the seeking of an answer to this question, I turn to the consideration of the colour-development process of the chromogens themselves, in which we see the oxidation-process, accelerated by the acid, play itself out before our eyes, and which are known to us, at least in the case of tyrosine, according to chemical structure.

## III. Chromogens.

### 1. The oxygen-receptivity of the chromogens.

#### a) Experimental technique.

That precisely oxidative processes are decisive for the formation of dark pigments from tyrosine or other chromogens — for this a whole series of facts speak, which have been found by earlier authors by means of various indirect methods.

Thus the higher oxygen-content of the eye-melanin compared with the protein was regarded as a sign of the oxidative origin of the eye-pigment (Landolt, 1899).

Also the suppression of melanin-formation by the presence of strong reducing agents, e.g. stannous chloride, agrees with the oxidative origin of the melanin (Hlasiwetz and Habermann, after Samuely, 1902), and the decolourisation of dark juices by tin-dust and hydrochloric acid (Gonnermann, 1907).

Furthermore, biological experiments have shown the failure of colour-change of developing insects when, by exclusion of water or other air, the access of oxygen had been reduced, e.g. in flies (Dewitz, 1902) or water-beetles (Megušar, 1906).

For the purpose of direct demonstration of oxygen-uptake I have made use of the following method:

Into a small, flat-standing little glass of 3 cm height and ½ cm diameter came the fluid to be examined, or the fluid-mixture to be examined; then a few drops of rat-blood were added, which by previous settling of the blood-corpuscles and dilution had taken on a light-red colour. If one regards the sample of a colourless chromogen, say of tyrosine, faintly coloured yellow-red by the dripping-in of the blood, by means of a spectroscope à vue directe, one sees very distinctly the two fine black absorption-stripes of the non-reduced blood. As is well known, the absorption-spectrum of the blood changes, when this loses the oxygen, in such a way that in place of the two fine stripes a broad band between these comes to appearance. The appearance of this band can therefore be regarded as an indicator of the giving-off of oxygen to the fluid, hence also of the progressive oxidation of this. At the same time it is, however, also a sign of the giving-off of carbonic acid on the part of the fluid to the blood, for the mentioned absorption-band holds precisely for the blood reduced by carbonic acid, while other reducing agents, e.g. carbon monoxide, again call forth other spectral-changes.

(For the demonstration of a giving-off of carbonic acid I have also thought of the use of the lime-milk test, but I have so far obtained no results with it.)

With strongly coloured chromogens this spectroscopic method will naturally be difficult to apply, since the field of view of the spectroscope is too much darkened to be able to distinguish the lines clearly; at least this will hold for the use of the not very sensitive spectroscope à vue directe.

The necessity of oxygen for a colour-development process in a fluid can, however, be shown in a very simple manner by alteration of the closure of our glass vessels. If the test-tubes are filled up to near their opening and corked tightly, then, in the case that an oxidation is in play, the colour-development must proceed essentially more slowly than in a just such, but only half-filled, little glass, still faster in one stopped merely with cotton-wool, and fastest in a vessel exposed quite openly to the air.

Corked, almost completely filled little glasses are also suitable for the observation of gas-bubbles emerging from the fluid. Among these, the ammonia is easily recognized by its characteristic odour and by the intense bluing of inserted litmus-paper.

#### b) Course of the experiment.

For the experiments on oxygen-receptivity there served first the tyrosine commercially obtainable in pure form, completely transparent and colourless in the water-dissolved state. If this is charged with a drop of tyrosinase, then the rapid entry of an intense colouration depends in fact on the kind of closure. Whereas corked samples of 2 cm³ each of tyrosine solution, after addition of 1 drop of basic *Agaricus*-tyrosinase, in corked state showed only a light-grey colour after 2 days, with bubbles rising up (Or.-Nr. 258), in the cotton-wool-stopped samples a deep violet was visible (Or.-Nr. 259). In corked samples, to which 1 drop of rat-blood had previously been added, there showed itself the broad reduction-band of the carbonic-acid blood (Or.-Nr. 260). This spectroscopic change had set in already after 1 day, but not on the afternoon of the experiment-day (Or.-Nr. 264). It also sets in in open samples (Or.-Nr. 265).

blood (Or.-Nr. 260). This spectroscopic change had already set in after 1 day, but not on the afternoon of the experimental day (Or.-Nr. 264). It also occurs in open samples (Or.-Nr. 265). Tyrosine without tyrosinase, open or corked, still shows after 2 days no reduction of the rat blood (Or.-Nr. 262), although the bands become more indistinct, more washed-out (Or.-Nr. 266, 267) and, in the corked samples, can bring about an approach toward the reduction band (Or.-Nr. 261).

Common-salt solution causes blood drops to display the characteristic two spectral lines for a considerable time longer (Or.-Nr. 263).

When using tyrosine samples that held only ½ cm³ each instead of 2 cm³, the reduction band on introduced blood could not be made distinct even after addition of *Agaricus* tyrosinase, since the bands in any case became weak after a short time (Or.-Nr. 268–272). In the tyrosine without tyrosinase they became broader and were distinct (Or.-Nr. 273). The distinctness of the bands depended on the solvent of the tyrosinase; they were still most distinct in water (Or.-Nr. 268), weak in ammonium sulphate (272) and sodium hydroxide solution (270), indistinct in common-salt solution (269), and entirely vanished in hydrogen peroxide (271).

The lines behave in entirely analogous fashion when the rat blood is added to the tyrosinases dissolved in these solvents without tyrosine (Or.-Nr. 274–278).

When the rat blood is added to the solutions without tyrosinase and without tyrosine, then in water, sodium hydroxide solution and common-salt solution the two bands remain distinct, in ammonium sulphate they become weak, and in hydrogen peroxide they have vanished (Or.-Nr. 279–283).

The differences in the rat blood demonstrable by the spectroscopic route can be recognized without a spectroscope from the colour change of the solutions. As is well known, oxygen-rich blood is of a more red, carbonic-acid-rich blood of a more blue colour. Correspondingly, the samples cited above which yield reduction bands are of a more carmine-red, those which display two bands of a more brick-red colour.

The loss of the spectral lines in the hydrogen peroxide solution after 1 day makes itself noticeable through the appearance of an apple-green colour of the blood (Or.-Nr. 282), which, even in the presence of a masking initial colour (Or.-Nr. 277) or of pigment formation (Or.-Nr. 271), makes itself felt in a dulling of the red tone.

After 8 days the blood has paled toward yellow in all samples, and in the samples with tyrosine and tyrosinase a striking dichroism appears:

In incident light, the samples charged with rat blood appear green in various nuances; in transmitted light, ochre-yellow, yellow-red to red-brown (Or.-Nr. 268–273), whereby the tones seem to depend on the solvent of the tyrosinase.

At this time the spectral lines of the blood were no longer to be seen, although no complete darkening of the middle portion of the spectrum had yet appeared. But there is indeed a very strong darkening of the violet-blue spectral region noticeable, such as is characteristic of the green pigments of the animal kingdom. But to this we shall only return when discussing those.

#### c) Experimental results.

According to our experiments, regarding the metabolism which the tyrosine undergoes under the influence of the tyrosinases, it can be stated with certainty that in this process it takes up oxygen from the surroundings. As cleavage products, carbonic acid and ammonia appear (also surmised by Czapek, 1913, p. 107). Further, from the colour sequence in the case of basic tyrosinase it is probable that water too is given off, which naturally cannot be demonstrated directly in the in any case watery solution. As we heard earlier, the addition of distilled water brings about a colour change toward blue. But such a change also occurs, in the further course of melanin formation with basic, red-tending tyrosinase, even without addition of water, although not always to the same degree.

The three cleavage products carbonic acid, ammonia and water agree completely with the formulae which Gonnermann (1900) sets up for the colouration of dark beet-juices, in which he had found homogentisic acid to be the colour-forming component, which on addition of alkali turns red, while through mineral acids the dark colour is not altered. Gonnermann assumes that this arises from the tyrosine, with the addition of water and oxygen, according to the following formula¹):

| Tyrosine | | 2 (H₂O) water, 3 oxygen |
|---|---|---|
| C₆H₄ . OH . CH₂ . CH . NH₂ . COOH | + 4 H + | 5 O = |
| C₆H₃(OH)₂ . CH₂ . COOH + NH₃ | + CO₂ + | 2 H₂O |
| Homogentisic acid&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Ammonia | Carbonic acid | Water |

> ¹) In the formula given by Gonnermann for homogentisic acid there occurs a sense-distorting misprint OH₂ instead of (OH)₂.

We are all the more entitled to make use of this formula for our experiments, since in testing the tyrosinases we have predominantly made use of tyrosine itself, whereas Gonnermann has it arise only through enzymatic cleavage of the protein; and besides, animal and plant tyrosine have, moreover, proved to be identical.

To be sure, the chromogen of beets and other plants would, according to Bertrand (1896), be completely identical with tyrosine. Czapek (1902) and Bertel (1902) know of the breakdown of tyrosine in roots and germinating plants into homogentisic acid upon stimulation.

With respect to the animal chromogens, this matter is still questionable. Gessard (1902) holds it not impossible that [the animal chromogens are] different from the plant ones, because the immunization of animals with plant tyrosinase acted inhibitingly only upon the plant enzyme, and likewise animal tyrosinase [acted] only upon animal enzyme. O. v. Fürth and E. Jerusalem (1907) could likewise win no immune serum from a rabbit pretreated with animal tyrosinase; O. v. Fürth and Schneider (1901) could, after thorough removal of all protein residues, detect no more tyrosine in lepidopteran chromogen by means of the Millon reaction, [and] therefore held the animal chromogen to be different from tyrosine. We have hitherto nowhere (also not, with Gortner, 1911, in the mealworm) missed the Millon reaction, which, however, may be traceable to a less thorough expulsion of the protein residues or to other accompanying circumstances. Yet v. Fürth told me, on my inquiry, that he by no means holds the presence of tyrosine in animal chromogen to be excluded. It thus for the present still remains an open question whether there is only one chromogen in pigment formation, which would be identical with tyrosine, or whether there are various chromogens, of which some perhaps do not contain tyrosine at all, but rather a related aromatic complex (cf. further below, hormone-sensitivity; Bloch, 1916).

At the beginning of our experiments we believed we could distinguish various chromogens according to the various colourations, until it turned out that chromogens warmed to nearly 100° become almost colourless without losing their oxygen- and ferment-sensitivity.

Inasmuch as I devote the next chapter to the ferment-sensitivity of the chromogens, I defer the discussion of the modifications in pigment formation to the chapter following upon that, in which the discussion 'of the hormone-sensitivity' of the chromogens is to take place.

Since in the animal body the artificial melanin prepared from tyrosine, just as the one arising from tyrosine, is nitrogen-containing (cf. v. Fürth and Jerusalem, 1907), whereas the homogentisic acid is nitrogen-free, the latter cannot, indeed, as a colouring component here appear as intermediate product, [but] can hardly be made responsible for the melanin formation.

According to Gortner (1911, II.) investigations, the tyrosine is, through autofermentation, transformed into tyrosol. This process splits off from the carboxyl (CO₂H) carbonic acid (CO₂) and replaces the amino group (NH₂) by hydroxyl (OH). Tyrosol is thus also a nitrogen-free cleavage product of tyrosine (like homogentisic acid). Upon addition of tyrosinase the colour turns from rose-red to, finally, black. Gortner concludes from this, probably with right, that the dark colour of the melanin-formation precursor first comes about upon entry of the nitrogen group into the reaction.

With all our experiments it stands in best agreement that the entry of a hydroxyl group favours, or even directly evokes, the red-colouration with the enzyme. The homogentisic acid too turns red in the presence of alkali (Gonnermann, 1900).

The entry of the hydroxyl group makes substances that are difficult to oxidize relatively easily oxidizable, as, e.g., upon the transition from phenylalanine to homogentisic acid (v. Fürth and Jerusalem, 1907).

According to the results of analyses of various authors, the transformation of the tyrosine into artificial melanin comes about without essential displacement of the proportions between nitrogen and carbon (1 : 9), merely through replacement of hydrogen by oxygen (ibid.). The nitrogen- or carbon-containing volatile cleavage products which arise upon the formation of the red precursor must thus always be excreted in corresponding proportion, or in part used again (e.g. when homogentisic acid forms the intermediate link).

### 2. The ferment-[enzyme-]sensitivity of the chromogens.
#### a) Experimental technique.

Since the tyrosinases are to be regarded as oxidative enzymes, a small quantity of tyrosinase must, in order to transform a great quantity of chromogen into melanin, have sufficient oxygen at its disposal.

This condition is fulfilled in practice already through the use of not too high fluid-columns and through the cotton-wool closure of the test-glasses.

In order to test whether in our experiments the sensitivity for small quantities of tyrosinase is really sufficient to be able to assume a catalytic effectiveness of the tyrosinases, no special apparatus, but only slight modifications of the experimental arrangement, were required.

Samples of 1 cm³ tyrosine solution each were charged with a rising number of drops of the same tyrosinase, and observed whether, in the final colour-result, a difference within the same samples appeared. Further, samples were set up with two tyrosinase drops, with one tyrosinase drop and one drop of the fluid serving as solvent for the same, finally with two drops of this fluid. If it is really a matter of ferment-action, then between the first and second samples no difference in the colour may come to light, while in the last case naturally no colouration appears. Were it, on the other hand, a matter of a participation of the tyrosinase in the quantitative composition of the melanin, then it would be to be expected that the mixed samples would retain, in colour, the middle between complete colouration and colourlessness.

In analogous manner one tested whether tyrosinases which, as a consequence of prior irradiation, exert an opposing influence upon the colouration process would, side by side in the same tyrosine sample, yield a mean colouration or a complete blackening.

These experiments were the necessary precondition for the further experimental arrangements, in which tyrosinases from two opposingly-acting pre-irradiations, up to nearly the limit of their effectiveness, each by itself, simultaneously added or freshly mixed, and also after longer mixing, were brought to act upon tyrosine in equal drop-number.

It was to be investigated here whether, with the dilution of the tyrosinase additionally arrived at herewith, all drops would attain a uniform result, or whether the various tyrosinases would still remain separated for some time and would therefore depend on chance, namely whether a just-sufficient quantity of the "going-on" tyrosinase-particles would fall on one sample [and] colour it out, while another sample, equipped with a "not-going-on" larger quantity, would not colour out. The result would then have to be the non-uniformity in the samples.

#### b) Course of the experiments.

If one brings into 1 cm³ tyrosine solution one drop of a basic *Agaricus* tyrosinase of strength 1 (Or.-Nr. 291), then the red-colouration sets in within the first hour, [and] the melanin formation follows. The same course is taken by an analogous experiment with one drop of strength ½ (Or.-Nr. 288). Freshly dissolved tyrosine without tyrosinase drops remains in the same period of time completely clear as water, even when one drop of sodium hydroxide solution of the same concentration as that prevailing in the *Agaricus* tyrosinase has been added (Or.-Nr. 292).

The same experiment can be carried out without the use of tyrosinases of various strength, in that on the one hand two drops of a tyrosinase of definite strength (½: Or.-Nr. 289), on the other hand one drop of the same tyrosinase and one drop of sodium hydroxide solution were instilled (Or.-Nr. 290). Both kinds of samples show on the next days the same blackening, while the control samples charged with two drops of sodium hydroxide solution remain clearly blue.

For mixing experiments with pre-irradiated tyrosinases there was used ¹/₄₀-*Agaricus*, since just then yellow-green, post-yellow and dark caused no colouration, [whereas] blue, purple, grey, black and white [caused] colouration, when two drops each on 1 cm³ tyrosine were contained.

If one drop of yellow-green and purple, or of post-yellow and blue, or of dark and grey were used, so that thus each sample received one drop of inactivated and one drop of activated tyrosinase, then colouration set in everywhere (Or.-Nr. 38).

The rapidity of the colouration is delayed in comparison with samples with two "going-on" drops. This does not lie in the heterogeneity of the pre-irradiation itself, since also the combination black plus white colours out just as rapidly as when two drops of black or two drops of white are used alone.

If two differently effective tyrosinases were first mixed in equal parts and two drops of them used, then colouration likewise set in, e.g. with post-yellow plus grey, with yellow-green plus purple (Or.-Nr. 40). Also one drop of this mixture still sufficed (Or.-Nr. 41), yet the colouration was distinctly slower.

The combination yellow-green plus purple behaves no differently in the course of the experiment when the tyrosinases of the yellow-green pre-irradiation are in the activated, and correspondingly those of the purple [pre-irradiation] in the inactivated, state (Or.-Nr. 46).

If the tyrosinases of yellow-green and purple were first mixed in equal parts and two drops of this mixture instilled, then there appeared, after half an hour, a striking non-simultaneity in the going-on of these samples. On the second experimental day, e.g., 2 out of 8 samples went on red; on the third day these were already grey-violet, while 3 others had gone on red, [and] the 3 remaining ones had stayed clear as water. On the fourth day 2 samples were still darker iron-violet, 4 of medium tone, 2 still pale lilac (Or.-Nr. 45; likewise 43, 44).

If the tyrosinases remained some days in mixture before they were used, then the heterogeneity of the samples vanishes, they all go on equally fast (Or.-Nr. 47).

Since, with rising pre-irradiation duration, a change with the tyrosinases, namely inactivation of the "yellow-green" pre-irradiated ones, sets in, the inactivation of the same can, with long duration of the pre-irradiation, be so far advanced that they are indeed still fully effective in two drops, but in one drop work essentially more slowly. Then the combination yellow-green plus purple also comes, without prior mixing, to heterogeneous results, inasmuch as many samples go on earlier, others later (Or.-Nr. 42).

If light-influenced tyrosinases were withdrawn from the specific light-influence, then they retained their specific effectiveness-state still longer, reacted on the tenth day of their later keeping in ordinary periodic daylight as on the last day of their pre-irradiation (Or.-Nr. 36), still on the 15th day (Or.-Nr. 46) and on the 17th day (Or.-Nr. 48).

As I have already mentioned in the discussion of the experimental sources (II, 1 a C), the heterogeneity of the individual particles with respect to their state of progress plays a different kind of role with regard to the appearance of various kinds of results in apparently equal experimental arrangement.

In particular, one series of experiments had alarmed us by its irregularity. There appeared always, alongside the expected going-on samples, those not going on. In order to clear up the irregularity, we wanted to make residues in the test-tubes — ferment-traces stemming from earlier experiments — responsible. But the use of completely new test-tubes, used for no ferment-experiments and freshly prepared by the glass-blower, yielded the same irregularities (Or.-Nr. 284–285). Then we thought of a thickening of the tyrosinase suspension, so that soon denser complexes, soon ones poor in tyrosinase, would be drawn up with the pipette. But the freshly filtered tyrosinase portion showed the same irregular behaviour (Or.-Nr. 286–287).

The tyrosinase portion happened to have been kept in a broad flask, in whose interior, in consequence of the slight transparency of thicker tyrosinase layers (*Agaricus*), it must have been dark. Otherwise mostly thin test-tubes were used for the keeping of the tyrosinases, in which the less thick fluid-layer was still translucent. On a tyrosinase portion kept in such a way, of the same preparation date as the one in the dark flask, the irregularities did not appear.

#### c) Experimental results.

Through pre-irradiation, activated and inactivated tyrosinases can thus, under equal light- and temperature-conditions, retain their state for at least a further two weeks.

Their coexistence is also possible in one and the same fluid, in that the inactivated tyrosinase at ordinary temperature does not inhibit the active one in its effectiveness.

If the active tyrosinase used is strong enough to set into action, by itself alone, a portion of pure tyrosine twice as large as that which corresponds to the one fed to the tyrosinase mixture, then the colouration result will be the same as if only the active tyrosinase had been added.

The presence of the inactivated tyrosinase accordingly exerts no other effect on the activated tyrosinase than its solvent without tyrosinase would exert in the same drop-quantity. If activated and non-activated tyrosinase are mixed, the two kinds remain separated for a short time still, at least half an hour, so that samples set up before this time show a differing behaviour with respect to the onset. The same result must occur when one part of the tyrosinase employed has been activated through the application of a light-factor, but another part has not been activated owing to a chance different position of the particles relative to the light-source, e.g. in the interior of a flask which was filled with a dark tyrosinase-solution.

If, as active tyrosinase, such a concentration was taken for mixing with non-active [tyrosinase] as lay close to the efficacy-limit, then upon the dropping-in of two such previously mixed drops into tyrosine-samples there at first appeared three kinds of samples: strongly coloured, slightly coloured, and entirely uncoloured, namely in the approximate ratio of 1 : 2 : 1. This corresponds to the simple probability of the falling of active and non-active tyrosinase-particles upon the drops employed. For in a drop of the mixed, but not entirely thoroughly mixed, tyrosinase there will namely be present either so many active tyrosinase-particles that they suffice for the onset, or there will not be present so many. These two kinds of drops now combine into the four cases: two drops sufficing for the onset; one drop sufficing for the onset and one not sufficing; one not sufficing and one sufficing; two not sufficing. Since the two middle combinations are practically the same, there arises the cited ratio 1 : 2 : 1. Those samples which contain two drops, of which each by itself would already just suffice for the colouration, will undergo the onset more quickly and deepen their colouration more rapidly than those in which only one just-sufficing [drop] was present, whereas those with two non-sufficing [drops] should not colour at all, if the limiting concentration was correctly hit and the inactivated tyrosinase by itself alone would really produce no colouration at all. In our experiments this was not quite achieved, since the tyrosinases employed as inactivated still had some efficacy after all, as became evident from the much later-appearing discolouration of control-samples which had been charged only with tyrosine and two drops of these inactivated tyrosinases. Since the drops withdrawn from the tyrosinase-mixture will contain not merely either the active or the inactive particles, we may therefore not expect a sharp separation of our combination-cases; but rather all transitional grades will occur. If finally the two tyrosinases are mixed for a longer time, then a complete thorough mixing sets in and there will then be obtained, upon use of two drops each, only uniform results, since each drop contains a proportionately equal number of particles of the active tyrosinase and of the non-active.

## 3. The Hormone-Susceptibility of the Chromogens.

### a) Experimental technique.

In order to be able to use the animal chromogens for colouration-experiments, one must prevent that, in the half-saturated ammonium sulphate, the colouration should set in spontaneously through traces of left-over tyrosinase. This Frl. [Miss] Dr. Brecher achieved in her pupa-experiments in a very simple way through boiling of the filtrate remaining after filtering off the precipitated tyrosinase-portion, which must contain the largest amount of the chromogen. We have later always retained this method, only with the precaution of warming only to 90° C in the water-bath, since it had shown itself that the boiling-temperature is able to bring about too far-reaching changes of the filtrate. On the other hand, brief warming of 70–90° C is also without influence on tyrosine, as far as its colourability under the influence of tyrosinases is concerned (cf. Or.-Nr. 85–98; 316–319), whereas these warming-degrees destroy the tyrosinase, hence suffice for its switching-off. On one occasion the experiment was also undertaken to obtain the chromogen free of ammonium sulphate, by bringing Filtrate I to evaporation. The ammonium sulphate then separates out crystalline above the chromogen remaining at the bottom of the test-tube [Epruvette], and can be removed by careful driving-off by means of a small glass rod. The left-over chromogen is dissolved in an ammonium-sulphate-free liquid (Or.-Nr. 293, *Dixippus*). The cumbersomeness of this method has, however, deterred us from its further use.

### b) Course of the experiment.

Through the warming to 90° there occurs coagulation of the egg-white-bodies [albumin-bodies] still present in Filtrate I, and one obtains, after their filtering-off, a clear, but mostly somewhat coloured chromogen-solution. If one charges samples of chromogens so treated with drops of various tyrosinases, then one notices that not in all samples does the same colouration take place.

In Table A are the experiments referring hereto compiled as Or.-Nr. 294–310, and for comparison the analogous experiments with tyrosine Or.-Nr. 311–322 appended.

In the case of the butterfly-pupae (Or.-Nr. 294–308), the blood dripping out from a puncture-wound was processed, in the case of mammals (Or.-Nr. 309–310) the comminuted skin. In the table, the species whose chromogen was used in the relevant experiment is designated at the beginning of each horizontal row, whereas the speciality of the tyrosinase [is designated] at the head of each vertical column. In order to recognise the influence of the chromogen on the colouration, one must therefore compare the colours obtained in its horizontal row for various tyrosinases. If, on the other hand, the influence of the tyrosinase is to be recognised, then one must look down the vertical column belonging to the corresponding tyrosinase. A glance at the compilation suffices to see that in each horizontal row the most varied colours are to be met with, whereas in each individual vertical column there occur only very limited deviations from an onset-colour characteristic for it. Tyrosine behaves just like the other chromogens; with the water-bright initial-state of the tyrosine, the uniformity of the onset-colours with one and the same tyrosinase emerges more [strongly] than with the use of various chromogens, which from the beginning deviate somewhat from one another in various colour-tones. In particular, with the use of greenish chromogen, the red onset-colour of the *Agaricus*-tyrosinase can be blunted into more yellow-orange, the violet [onset-colour] of the *Pieris*-tyrosinase into cinnabar (Or.-Nr. 304–305).

The chromogens of the warm-blooded animals showed only slight colourability with the tyrosinases; coloured and white skin-spots, as well as skins of albinotic animals, behaved alike in this respect.

### c) Experimental results.

The "onset-colour" of the chromogens is independent of their belonging to a particular animal-species; merely the admixture of the onset-colour to an already-present initial-colour of the chromogen-solution conditions deviations in the colour-tones of various chromogen-species when a tyrosinase of like kind is used.

For the onset-colour, accordingly, the tyrosinase employed is alone decisive.

From the onset-colour of the tyrosinase in tyrosine we can predict what colour a chromogen of another provenance will assume under the influence of the same tyrosinase. This result agrees entirely with the changes of the onset-colour, discussed in earlier chapters, in the case of light-changed (Tab. A–D), in the case of temperature-changed tyrosinases (Table E) and in the case of reaction-changed [ones] (Table F). In each case the tyrosinase forces the chromogen to that "onset-colour" which corresponds to the tyrosinase-state.

For the explanation of the various colours it is therefore not yet necessary to assume various chromogens in the various species; the weak initial-colours can easily rest upon admixtures of other substances (cf. below p. 131).

Since we are now in a position, through alteration of the reaction-state, to grade the onset-colours of one and the same tyrosinase, it also suffices to assume a single kind of tyrosinase in the various invertebrate animal-species, which, just like the plant-tyrosinase, obtains the capacity for colouration in various tones only through admixture of inorganic substances.

Such substances could, according to the terminology developed in connection with Starling (Czapek, 1913), be designated as "hormones" or stimulus-substances.

We then obtain, as a brief formulation of our results on the question whether the difference of the colours rests upon various chromogens, upon various tyrosinases, or upon other circumstances, the following:

One chromogen, one tyrosinase, various hormones.

With respect to the warm-blooded vertebrates (perhaps the vertebrates in general) I should like, with reference to the slight tyrosinase-susceptibility of their skin-chromogens, to leave it still undecided whether we are not here dealing with another colour-forming pair, namely an oxidase active upon another chromogen. Thus Bloch (1916) claims to have demonstrated, in coloured vertebrate-skin, a "Dopa"-oxidase, which is absent from white skin-spots and albinos and gives colouration only with dioxyphenylalanine ("Dopa"), whereas tyrosine, homogentisic acid, etc. remain uncoloured. That would naturally not exclude that we might, with the proposition: one chromogen, one tyrosinase, various hormones, again explain the differences of the colours within the warm-blooded animals (or vertebrates in general). It would be only to set, in place of the tyrosine, the dioxyphenylalanine, in place of the tyrosinase the "Dopa"-oxidase. The hormones could remain the same for analogous colours. The difference in the chromogens and in the tyrosinases themselves cannot be made responsible for the various colours, since indeed both colour-giving combinations are able to bring about the black colouration, [and] since also invertebrates and vertebrates cannot be distinguished as whole groups by colour, but rather all colours can occur within each group, indeed mostly within each species, as a racial characteristic.

As a particular example, reference may be made to the hair-colour of humans, whose differences Wurster (1887) had already tried to trace back to reaction-states. To the explanation of racial differences we shall come back later.

## IV. Pigments.

### 1. The Melanins as Precipitation-products.

#### a) Experimental technique.

With the demonstration of the tyrosine- or other chromogen-substance-change, the chemical composition of the pigments coming to deposition in the animal-body is by no means settled. Already the analysis of finished animal-pigments yields various percentage-values for carbon and hydrogen, for oxygen and nitrogen, for sulphur and iron. The common criterion of the melanins is constituted, according to Hofmeister, by the ratio of the atom-numbers N : H : C = 1 : 5 : 5; their cleavage-products, namely on potash-fusion indole and skatole, make them appear as heterocyclic compounds (cf. on this O. v. Fürth, 1904 with Lit. [literature]). With the chemical analysis we have not occupied ourselves; only the chemist by profession can here procure further insight into the constitution of the colour-substances.

I should like in this section to communicate only a few observations on the physical behaviour of arising pigments, which can become of interest for the explanation of biological colourations. The experimental technique consists, on the one hand (α), in the precipitation of self-forming colour-substances in narrow test-tubes [Epruvetten] through chemicals, or (β) repeated filtering; on the other hand (γ) in the long-lasting rest of tyrosinase-charged chromogens of colour-modifications of one and the same animal-species and observation of the processes playing out above the liquid-level upon slow evaporation under artificial light.

#### b) Course of the experiment.

α) If tyrosine-samples of 0.2 cm³ each are treated with active tyrosinase (Or.-Nr. 121, *Agaricus*), then after a short time red tones appear, which, when the test-tubes are at rest, proceed from the bottom, because the tyrosinase-drop has settled down there. On shaking, the colouration appears diffuse in the whole space. If now to the already coloured liquid 0.6 cm³ each of distilled water, or half-percent common-salt-solution, or half-per-mille soda-solution is added, then in the two first cases at first only an insignificant colour-change towards blue will appear, in the latter [case] at once a diffuse yellow-brown colour will set in. Whereas in the distilled water (Or.-Nr. 122) the colour appears darker at the bottom and ever lighter towards the surface, in the common-salt-solution (Or.-Nr. 123) the reverse behaviour is to be noticed. After six weeks the picture had quite changed: in the distilled water there is now a diffuse brown colour, whereas in the physiological common-salt-solution dark-brown flakes have settled at the bottom. In the caustic-soda [Natronlauge] there prevails a diffuse light-yellow colouration (Or.-Nr. 124).

The rising of the darker-coloured liquid in the common-salt-solution and its sinking in the distilled water can also be noticed upon direct addition of 1 cm³ of these solution-quantities to 1 cm³ each of Filtrate I of *Agaricus* (Or.-Nr. 125–128), which, by its dark-chocolate-brown colour, already contains chromogen changed by the tyrosinase of the fungus. These samples remain, just as does the analogous sample appearing at once diffuse yellow-brown with 0.05% caustic-soda (Or.-Nr. 128), unprecipitated even after several months; all samples have meanwhile [acquired] the same appearance as those obtained in caustic-soda.

Samples treated with concentrated caustic-soda, however, show the appearance of a fibrous-flocculent brown suspension, which, however, is again dissolved, so that even after several months a uniform liquid of lighter yellow colouration than with the previously mentioned agents is present (Or.-Nr. 129).

Permanent precipitations set in upon use of hydrogen peroxide (10%), concentrated sulphuric acid, normal hydrochloric acid, or saturated ammonium-sulphate-solution.

Upon addition of an equal quantity of the hydrogen-peroxide-solution, the upper part of the liquid is at first darker, but in the course of several months there occurs complete settling of a dark-brown substance at the bottom of the test-tube, while the liquid situated above [it] becomes completely clear and water-bright (Or.-Nr. 134).

Upon addition of concentrated sulphuric acid the lower test-tube-half at first remains brown-coloured, but in the upper [half] a flocculent precipitation of a brown mass appears, whereby the flakes range themselves into net-like masses; after months the net-like mass condenses ever more, without it coming in the lower part of the test-tube to a complete clarification (Or.-Nr. 131).

Normal hydrochloric acid soon gives a magnificent net-like flocculation, which, with time, under complete liquid-clarification brings the entire pigment-quantity to separation (Or.-Nr. 132).

With ammonium sulphate there occurs in the whole test-tube a fibrous-flocculent separation, which likewise, under complete clarification, leads to a finer net-like pigment-precipitation (Or.-Nr. 135).

Transient star-like precipitations occur with nitric acid (Or.-Nr. 130), whereas with ammonia no precipitation was observed (Or.-Nr. 133); about these experiments we shall speak still in the next section, where it concerns the red pigment-stages.

If we use, in place of the *Agaricus*-filtrate, samples of tyrosine that have just become violet, to which butterfly-pupa-tyrosinase had been added, then we notice, upon addition of distilled water, again the sinking of the colour, with 0.5% common-salt-solution its rising, in 0.05% caustic-soda diffuse spreading (Or.-Nr. 245–248); in concentrated caustic-soda the coloured layer rises (Or.-Nr. 249; *Pieris brassicae*). Concentrated sulphuric acid produces a fine-flocculent, brown suspension with a tendency to clumping (Or.-Nr. 252). Normal hydrochloric acid gives beautiful star-shaped precipitations (Or.-Nr. 253). In both cases the liquid becomes already by the next day completely water-bright.

Concentrated caustic-soda (Or.-Nr. 249, *Pieris*; 230, *Saturnia*) and nitric acid (Or.-Nr. 251, *Pieris*) give incomplete flocculation under yellowing of the liquid; ammonia (Or.-Nr. 254, *Pieris*) dissolves without preceding precipitation.

In the large Viennese night-peacock-moth, *Saturnia pyri*, the pupa-tyrosinase showed, in agreement with the beautiful blue colouration of the onset tyrosine-samples, a strongly acid reaction. Here the addition of physiological common-salt-solution already sufficed to bring about a fibrous separation of the violet pigment under clarification of the liquid (Or.-Nr. 228), whereas analogous samples with distilled water remained lilac-coloured (Or.-Nr. 227), [and] with 0.05% caustic-soda became yellowish-grey (Or.-Nr. 229).

β) If the skins of agouti-coloured migratory-rats, *Mus decumanus*, were likewise pre-processed with a view to the production of an oxidase, as otherwise lower animals [are], the following conditions showed themselves when a division of the skins according to various zones had been undertaken (Or.-Nr. 342–347).

The dorsal half yielded a very dark expressed-juice [Preßsaft] in the added physiological common-salt-solution; treated with ammonium sulphate, [it gave] likewise still an opaque, dark filtrate. The filter-residue taken up again with 0.05 caustic-soda deposited after three days a strong black precipitate under complete clarification of the liquid, so that upon careful decanting onto a filter there could now be obtained a completely water-clear filtrate and a dark, firm residue.

In the opposite way behaved the ventral halves. The initially light expressed-juice yielded with ammonium sulphate a clear, somewhat yellowish filtrate; in return, the residue taken up again was turbid, grey, without distinct separation into sediment and liquid, so that upon decanting and renewed filtering again only a yellowish-grey, opalescent liquid was obtained.

A similar, but less striking difference was yielded by skin-portions which had been selected according to their greater or lesser pigmentation from the same rats. And indeed, as was to be foreseen, the more strongly pigmented skin-spots correspond γ) The processing of whole specimens of the stick insect *Dixippus morosus*, for the purpose of obtaining tyrosinases and chromogens, was carried out with selection of uniform colouration within each batch processed together. Since the various colour-modifications of *Dixippus* in their connection with the surrounding colour and their heritability form the subject of further investigations, which will hardly be concluded so soon — for quite complicated conditions appear to be present — we shall for the present only briefly cite those phenomena which let us recognise differences in the pigments of the colour-modifications.

1. Already in the preparation of the pressed juices from the whole animals, which had previously been starved in order to remove all faeces, differences appeared in the colour of the fluid obtained from each colour-modification. These colours can be applied with a watercolour brush onto drawing paper and yield characteristic colour-tones which, in their colour value, agree with the general colouration of the living modifications.

2. If the press-residues are placed into sulphuric ether, the ether is coloured with various colours, though not every modification has its own colour in this respect. After pouring off the ether, more can be poured on repeatedly, and it colours itself each time intensely and with the same colour as the first time.

3. The chromogen-containing filtrates obtained by precipitation of the press-juices with ammonium sulphate and filtering off of the tyrosinase-richer residue (Filtrate I, and after a renewed ammonium-sulphate washing of the residue, Filtrate II) likewise have different colours in the different modifications, though this colour is soon overlaid by darkening as a consequence of the further-advancing melanin-formation process.

4. If tyrosine samples are treated with the tyrosinases of various modifications, the strength of melanin-formation in the test-glass changes with the modification used (cf. for points 1.–4. the experimental protocols, Or.-No. 368–375). The order in which the darkening deepens does not, however, remain the same with increasing age of the tyrosinase, as can be gathered from experiments Or.-No. 323, 324–326; 340–341 for two-, three-, and four-week-old tyrosinases. The colours of the samples also change with longer standing of the same experiment; a "greening" can set in, to which we shall return when discussing the green pigments.

5. A wholly unexpected, and hitherto still unexplained, observation I made on samples which formed melanin through the action of the tyrosinases on the chromogens decolourised by heating. Apart from the order of the colour-deepening, which depended on the tyrosinase-modification in the same way as in the use of tyrosine, one could, in artificial light, when one cautiously raised the samples up against the incandescent lamp, notice on the various test-tubes, above the darkening-colouring fluids, delicate figures which showed a quite varied appearance: in some samples it was a fine-rayed, quite broad breath of blue colour; in others there appeared ring-shaped zones of white stripes or white spots. These latter were now punctiform, now more strongly developed; the former now club-shaped, now needle-shaped in their development. These latter needles could also show the blue colour of the breath. The samples, which at first stood next to one another arranged according to the tyrosinases, appeared, with respect to these figures, to stand in disorder. The picture changed at once, however, as soon as I arranged them according to the chromogens used: now all the samples with the same figure came to stand next to one another, without any exception. The differences of the individual modifications expressed themselves so distinctly in these figures that Frl. Dr. Brecher could at once recognise, from the samples brought back into disorder, those belonging to the same modification. The characteristic behaviour of each modification is cited in experiments Or.-No. 327–333. In order to convince myself still further of the constancy of these appearances, I used chromogen-samples belonging to the various modifications, which had all been charged with an *Agaricus*-tyrosinase of the same strength (Or.-No. 334–338). Again, for each chromogen-modification its characteristic figure appeared, so that I could, without heeding the labels, diagnose the modification used.

It must, however, be remarked here that one particular modification is distinguished from all the other chromogens of *Dixippus* precisely by the absence of any figure (it is "brown 2", Or.-No. 328 and 335). In order to see whether this sample too could be recognised as such, I charged tyrosine samples with the same tyrosinase-concentration of the *Agaricus* (Or.-No. 339). A difference showed itself insofar as, in these samples, a more or less coherent dark ring, doubtless of dark pigment, had deposited itself above the fluid, whereas above the chromogen of "brown 2" no deposit at all was to be seen. With advancing evaporation, more and more such rings were deposited above the tyrosine-solution, whereas with the chromogens the deposit took place at the bottom of the test-tube.

### c) Experimental results.

α) The artificial melanins forming out of tyrosine under the influence of tyrosinases behave, with respect to their sinking or rising in various fluids, to their slow mixing with these, to their precipitability through chemicals, and to the precipitation-forms occurring thereby, just as do those forming out of the natural chromogens.

They thus agree well with one another, to a high degree, in specific weight, diffusion-velocity, ionisation, and particle-form.

The specific weight is greater than that of distilled water, thus >1, and could be more exactly determined, even for small quantities, by the suspension method, on account of the slowness of the mixing. For the present work this question has had no interest. Important is the easy precipitability of the melanins by acids, where, especially in view of the processes in the living organism, the effectiveness of common salt in a weakly acid solution is interesting, because already the physiological concentration in the test-tube precipitates it. The precipitation by relatively low concentrations of hydrogen superoxide too may gain significance for the explanation of the fermentative processes (cf. Adler, Senter, Ewald, etc.).

The net-like and star-like forms occurring during the precipitation recall the coagulation of fibrin, in which it likewise comes to a chaining-together of suspended particles. To this peculiarity little attention has hitherto been given on the part of physicists and chemists; only in the very most recent time have Diesselhorst and Freundlich (1916) addressed fibrin as an anisotropic, amorphous-solid substance, after this aggregate-state had been established for another body, vanadium pentoxide (Diesselhorst, Freundlich and Leonhardt, 1915; Diesselhorst and Freundlich, 1915, 1916; Freundlich, 1916).

The said researchers see the cause of the lining-up of the particles along preferred directions in the elongated shape of the molecules, which in the amorphous state line up as wholes, whereas, according to the new views on crystallisation, the molecules undergo, in crystallising, a far-reaching subdivision, and for the lining-up only the atomic occupation of the space-lattice comes into consideration. Vanadium pentoxide becomes doubly-refracting on streaming, because thereby the molecules are all set with their long axis into the direction of the stream, but loses, on the cessation of the streaming, the double-refraction again, because the molecules, now subjected only to the undirected Brownian molecular-motion, swirl about in disorder with their long axes in all directions. In the transition into the solid state, owing to the elongated shape of the molecules, their chaining would become more intimate if they come to deposition with their long axes parallel or one behind the other, than if they abut perpendicularly to one another; and it comes therefore to the formation of fibres, nets, and stars.

β) If the dark pigments of artificial as of natural kind behave similarly to one another in their physical qualities, yet even within one and the same animal significant quantitative differences in precipitation-behaviour can be demonstrated, according as strongly or weakly pigmented parts come to be processed.

This is shown to us by the rat experiments, in which it is a matter of the progressive dark-colouring of the press-juice from coloured or uncoloured skin-areas, whereby no addition of tyrosine had taken place, so that only the natural chromogen took part.

By far the strongest was the precipitation in the dorsal zone, the weakest in the ventral. Selected pigmented skin-areas behaved similarly to the dorsal, selected non-pigmented ones similarly to the ventral. An intermediate position was taken by anterior or posterior animal-halves, onto each of which there had to fall half dorsal, half ventral, that is, half coloured, half uncoloured areas.

γ) To still stronger, and not merely quantitative, expression came the differences in the dark pigments in the use of colour-modifications of one and the same animal species, namely the *Dixippus morosus*.

1. The usability of the extracts of whole animals as "watercolours" of various toning shows that we have to do at least with different quantity-ratios of pigments.

2. The possibility, by means of sulphuric ether, of obtaining from the various colour-modifications (at least) two differently-coloured extracts, but not as many as there were colour-modifications described, speaks for this, that differences lie in the ether-insoluble dark, or also coloured, pigments themselves.

3. In the same sense, the various tones of the chromogens separated from the tyrosinase-portion by ammonium-sulphate half-saturation — Filtrate I and Filtrate II — speak for a plurality of water-soluble pigments. The rapid blackening of Filtrate I is doubtless again to be referred to the acidifying action of the ammonium sulphate.

4. Large differences of a quantitative nature result from the melanin-formation experiments in the test-tube with regard to the spreading of the dark pigment and its formation-capacity in the various colour-modifications.

5. Remarkable qualitative differences are the precipitation-figures appearing above the fluid-level with the chromogens of various colour-modifications, the nature of which has not been further investigated. Most probably it is a matter of crystals or myelin-forms, which, according to the simultaneous presence of other substances, are influenced by these as "solution-companions". In no case are they moulds, which would occur in various growth-forms, for we have had occasion to observe many times mould-infested chromogen-samples, which always — including with the chromogens of the *Dixippus* modifications — had a quite different appearance.

## 2. The red pigments as intermediate products.

### a) Experimental technique.

For the prevention of the passage of red tyrosine-samples into darker products, good corking of the filled samples was used. Otherwise there is nothing new to remark concerning the technique.

### b) Course of the experiments.

With spontaneously red-become tyrosine, similar experiments were carried out as before with the samples engaged in artificial melanin-formation (Or.-No. 348–357). The course of the experiment differs from the artificial melanin-formation only in this, that in general only very sparse little-skin-like precipitations come about, if at all. The "red tyrosine" is also not able to transfer its state to non-red tyrosine, of which one might perhaps have thought (Or.-No. 357–358).

In concentrated sodium hydroxide solution (Or.-No. 351), ammonia (Or.-No. 355), and hydrogen superoxide (Or.-No. 356), the red tyrosine retains its orange-red colour; nitric acid does not change the colour but flocculates it out (Or.-No. 352), whereas hydrochloric acid (Or.-No. 354) makes the colour of the flocks more vivid and sulphuric acid transforms it into iron-violet (Or.-No. 353).

Orange-red flocks arise also on the action of nitric acid on the *Agaricus*-melanin forming (Or.-No. 130), or on tyrosine induced to melanin-formation by pupa-tyrosinase (Or.-No. 251, *Pieris*). Yet the red flocks can be dissolved again in nitric acid, whereby, however, no red, but a greenish-yellow, only weakly-coloured solution arises, whereas concentrated sodium hydroxide solution holds the *Agaricus*-melanin (Or.-No. 133) dissolved with a yellow-red, the pupa-melanin (Or.-No. 249) with a "blond" colour; ammonia behaves likewise (Or.-No. 254).

If the excess of nitric acid is avoided, then the red flocks can be permanently preserved, and the difference between their colour and that of the fluid contrasts distinctly (Or.-No. 178 to 183), whereby it is indifferent whether the tyrosinase used was dissolved in soda, water, common salt, ammonium sulphate, or hydrogen superoxide.

With the amount of nitric acid one can go down so far that only a single drop is present to 1 cm³ of tyrosine-solution, without impairing the end-result. If the nitric acid is so strongly diluted that it gives but a barely acid reaction, then indeed precipitation and green-colouring of the fluid do not set in, but the red-brown colour-stage of the tyrosine remains preserved longer and passes, instead of into black (Or.-No. 189), into a greenish brown (Or.-No. 190).

The nitric-acid experiments just cited are carried out on samples which had not yet passed beyond the red-stage of the tyrosine. If samples are taken which have already become violet, then, on the addition of a single drop of concentrated nitric acid to 1 cm³ of tyrosine, a change of the violet into a greener colouration sets in, and red-brown flocks are deposited, whereas the fluid takes on no greenish colour, but remains brownish (Or.-No. 176).

### c) Experimental results.

Yellow- to brown-red products we have encountered in our experiments in eight different ways:

1. With spontaneous colour-change of long-dissolved tyrosine (S. 104).

2. Already after two days on holding a freshly prepared tyrosine-solution in the thermostat at 35—40° C (S. 104).

3. With the action of strong *Agaricus*-tyrosinase on tyrosine-solution as transition-stage to darker tones (S. 108).

4. With the action of bases on incipient butterfly-tyrosinases as transition-stage to darker tones (S. 110).

5. With the action of light of definite wavelengths on slowly incipient tyrosinases as transition-stages to darker tones (S. 95).

6. With airtight corking of such samples as lasting colour (S. 95).

7. With the action of nitric acid on incipient tyrosine-samples (S. 113).

8. With the action of nitric acid on already-formed melanin (S. 136).

All these cases already permit, according to the results thus far summarised together, a uniform conception of the red substance as an intermediate stage between chromogen and melanin, as I should now like to set forth.

First, the spontaneous colour-change of the tyrosine (1) is certainly not otherwise to be conceived than as a slow proceeding of the same process which at temperature-raising (2) goes on more rapidly, but undergoes still an essentially stronger acceleration through a corresponding enzyme, namely the tyrosinase (3).

Since acid accelerates this process still more, it often suppresses the red-stage, which first comes into appearance again with basic additions to such acid tyrosinases, e.g. those of the butterflies (4). On a raising of the basicity rests also, as was shown, the "inhibitory action" of certain light-rays, which therefore can favour the longer lingering of the red-stage (5). If through exclusion of oxygen the further proceeding of the oxidation is prevented altogether, then it remains at the red colouration (6).

But why does nitric acid inhibit the melanisation (7), nay, is even able to transform the already-formed black product back again into a red one (8)? This must lie in a specific colour-action of the nitration, which indeed also, in protein-cleavage, yields the orange-red-coloured xanthoprotein, well known as an albumen-test.

## 3. The yellow-green pigments as animal own-products.

### a) Experimental technique.

For the characterisation of the yellow-green pigments of the animals, which cannot directly be referred to plant chlorophyll, I (Przibram, 1906) have described several reactions, which we have now again brought into application. These are: (I) boiling of ethereal or alcoholic extracts with an equal quantity of alcoholic potassium-hydroxide solution; (II) addition of one to two drops of concentrated sulphuric acid, or (III) of fuming nitric acid, to 1 cm³ of dye-solution.

In a later publication (Przibram, 1913), the spectroscopic examination too had been used. For lack of a suitable spectroscope, we have for the present deferred the further use of this method for the exact determination of the spectral lines, and have contented ourselves with the establishment of chlorophyll-bands and xanthophyll-absorption with a small spectroscope à vue directe. Instead, it was tested, by shaking-out of alcoholic solutions with benzine, into which solvent the dyes of the animals thereby pass over, and the comparison was drawn with the known behaviour of the plant dyes, chlorophyll and xanthophyll. Since often only small quantities of animal dyes are available, the shaking-out was so carried out, that into a small flat-standing little glass 1 cm³ of the dye to be tested, dissolved in alcohol, was poured, then the same quantity of benzine was shaken upon it, and the content of the now-corked test-tube, by repeated turning-over, was shaken through one another. It thereby proved favourable to add to the alcoholic solution beforehand a drop of sulphuric ether, since otherwise no separation into the various colours came about.

## b) Course of the experiment

A brief summary of the experiments on the nature of the yellow and green colouring matters, in connection with earlier statements, is given in Table G. The more detailed data on the animal and plant species used are to be seen from the appended orientation numbers, which refer to our Table A, and from the literature references.

In gutted stick insects [Stabheuschrecken], upon shaking-out with benzene, the colouring matter extractable by ether-alcohol divided into a greener alcohol portion and a yellower benzene portion; in the case of carrots only the latter is present, whereas in old cabbage leaves the benzene portion becomes green and the alcohol portion yellow. Both portions were green in young bramble leaves [Brombeerblätter] and in the entrails — consisting of gut and eggs — of stick insects, both yellow in the blood of butterfly pupae [Schmetterlingspuppen]. Finally, only the alcohol portion remained yellow-coloured in the flesh-eating praying mantises [Fangheuschrecken] and in the investigated vertebrate skins. Chlorophyll bands were shown only by the green plants, by the egg–gut portion of the stick insects, and in minimal intimations by the wholly extracted stick insects, without regard to their general colouration. The absorption of the blue end of the spectrum, which is strong and characteristic of the xanthophyll group, was shown by all the yellow extracts.

Green and brown *Dixippus* give, in the ether extract, at first a green, or rather more brownish, solution colour; on the addition of tyrosinase, with brownish-green specimens the green colour becomes greenish-brown (Or.-No. 376), and the brownish becomes yellow-brown (Or.-No. 377); but later, in both cases, a brown precipitate falls out and the liquid above it appears clear, and indeed green. When to these same sulphur-ether extracts an equal quantity of ammonium sulphate was added and the mixture shaken, the liquid cleared; at about the half-height of the liquid orange-red flecks settled out, above them a green ring, and at the surface black dots, again without distinction between green or brown *Dixippus* (Or.-No. 378–379).

In the case of butterfly blood, Miss Dr. Brecher first succeeded in 1917 in finding differences in the blood colour of pupal-colour modifications of the Cabbage White [Kohlweißling], in that wholly green pupae exhibit a distinctly greener blood than non-green ones. When the blood, half of which had been treated with ammonium sulphate, was freed by filtration from the precipitated tyrosinase portion and the filtrate warmed to 90°, there formed under decolourisation a coagulum. Brought onto a filter and allowed to dry, the residue formed a brownish-white smear. When this was dissolved in ether or alcohol, the solvent again took on that colour which the blood had originally possessed, whereas from the aqueous solution no colouring matter had been given off into the other solvents.

We have applied a similar procedure to a series of butterflies and observed an analogous course, as appears from the small Table H concerning reactivated lipochromes (only in *Dilina tiliae* did the experiment not succeed, perhaps because of an already too far-reaching alteration of the chromogen).

The original colours re-established themselves, whether the differences rested upon modifications of the pupal colour — e.g. the black or red-brown pupa of the male of the Emperor moth [Nachtpfauenauge] *S. pyri* — or upon sexual difference, e.g. in the red-brown pupae of the male and female of the same species, or upon species difference, as in the females of the closely related hawkmoths [Schwärmer] *Smerinthus ocellatus* and *Sm. populi*.

On this occasion let it be mentioned that we were able repeatedly to observe the sexual difference of the pupal-blood colour found by Dewitz (1908, 1912, 1916) and Steche (1912), but that exceptions occur within one species, e.g. a female of the Privet Hawkmoth [Ligusterschwärmer] (Or.-No. 435) possessed yellow instead of grass-green blood; above all, however, the colour modifications of the pupae, which can entirely blur or suppress the sexual differences when they do not run parallel to them, as Poulton (1885) had supposed for the caterpillars of *Chaerocampa elpenor*. We shall return to this in later publications which will deal in particular with the pupal colouration.

Since in the pupal experiments a reappearance of green colouration came about even when uncooked chromogen had been strongly darkened by the tyrosinase contained in it, but the melanin had in time been precipitated by the ammonium sulphate — furthermore also in tyrosine samples after the addition of tyrosinase, as we have heard in the chapter on reaction-sensitivity, "greening" occurs under definite conditions — we attempted to determine whether these greened samples would yield reactions identical with those of the lipochromes. One cannot conclude beforehand, from the nitrogen-freedom of the lipochromes and the nitrogen-content of the tyrosine and of the melanin arising from it, the impossibility of a same origin-substance, since the red stage, which often inserts itself between tyrosine and melanin, can be nitrogen-free (cf. III, 1e), and the constitution of the melanins is in general not yet, as regards structure, established. These experiments, conducted on *Dixippus*, have however up to now taken no satisfactory course, since the "greened" samples available contained far too little colouring matter. On boiling the aqueous tyrosine sample with alcoholic potassium hydroxide [Kalilauge], adding nitric acid or sulphuric acid, the green colour vanished at once and, with clarification, an orange-brown precipitate was deposited (Or.-No. 386). It did not succeed in transferring the green colouring matter into ether or alcohol. On adding these substances to the aqueous sample, the coloured tyrosine fell out; if the water was first brought to evaporation, the oily residue remaining behind did not dissolve in ether or alcohol and also gave off no colouring matter to them. There was far too little colouring matter present for us to have been able to undertake the coagulation process described above for the recovery of the colouring matter from the filter residue. On adding 75% alcohol and shaking with benzene, the latter remained uncoloured, with foam formation (Or.-No. 394). Exactly the same result is obtained when using ammonium-sulphate filtrate I (Or.-No. 395) or II (Or.-No. 396).

## c) Results of the experiments

Yellow to yellow-green colouring matters can be obtained from land animals in the following ways:

1. By extraction from blood, gutted animals, or animal skins of corresponding colour in physiological aqueous solution, "watercolours of nature" (p. 132).

2. By clarification, on longer standing, of the chromogen portion that has been deprived — after ammonium-sulphate half-saturation — of the oxydase fraction (p. 140).

3. By the addition of tyrosinase with "green attack-colour" [Angehfarbe], i.e. probably at the minimum of action of every tyrosinase (p. 112).

4. By extraction from blood, gutted animals, or animal skins of corresponding colour by means of sulphur-ether or alcohol, to which it is best that a few drops of ether be admixed (p. 132, 139).

5. By heating the ammonium-sulphate filtrate to 90° and dissolving the dried brownish-whitish coagulum in ether or alcohol (p. 139, 140).

6. By the addition of tyrosinase to the ether solution, whereby the separation of the dark melanin and the clarification of the coloured solution come about (p. 139).

These widely distributed yellow and green colouring matters of animals alter, in contrast to the colour-stable plant chlorophyll, their colour easily on boiling with alcoholic potassium hydroxide, on the addition of fuming nitric acid or concentrated sulphuric acid.

The latter calls forth brown to violet tones, similarly as in plant fatty colouring matters, to which the "animal green" [Tiergrün] is also related in the spectrum.

On shaking alcoholic extracts with benzene, the typical behaviour of chlorophyll was never observed in animals, except when plant food, as for example in the gut–egg portion of *Dixippus*, was certainly present. Only in this case did the benzene take on a more vividly green, the alcohol a less green colour.

Otherwise either a yellow colouring matter went into solution into the benzene while the alcohol remained green, e.g. in gutted *Dixippus*- or pupa-skins, or the benzene remained entirely uncoloured, as in the mantids and vertebrate skins, or no separation of the yellow colour took place, as in the blood of many butterfly pupae.

Yellow benzene-colouration would allow inference to carotin, yellow alcohol-colouration to xanthophyll, no separation to a mixture of both substances.

According to Schunck (1904), the yellow colouring matter of the hen's egg-yolk is L.-xanthophyll, which however differs from the plant one by its different melting point, as Willstätter and Escher (1911–12) found, who therefore separate it off as "lutein." The same colouring matter as in the egg also colours the fat and serum of hens, according to Palmer's (1915) investigation, with which therefore our experiments on the skin extracts in the hen also agree.

On the other hand, the colouring substance of cow's milk (butter), of cow fat, corpus luteum, and cow serum is said to be carotin (Palmer 1915). Since Palmer judges less according to chemical properties than according to colorimetric, spectroscopic, and physiological (feeding-) experiments, we are not informed whether this substance is really identical with the plant carotin, namely whether it does not differ from it in melting point just as lutein differs from L.-xanthophyll, which would not be excluded, since one has to picture the xanthophyll as an oxidation product of the carotin (carotin = C₄₀H₅₆; xanthophyll = C₄₀H₅₆O₂).

Analogously, the animal carotin could be an oxidation product of the lutein.

When Palmer (1915) observed, on feeding xanthophyll-rich maize food to hens, an increase of the yellow yolk colour of their eggs, it would first have to be ascertained, through the melting-point determination, whether really lutein was present in increased amount, or whether there was not a "vital colouration" through xanthophyll, as indeed Palmer himself refers to the experiments of Riddle (1910), who saw hens fed with Sudan red lay Sudan-coloured eggs. Palmer's experiments encompassed far too few eggs to permit at all the conclusion drawn by him, that the plant xanthophyll is directly to blame for the yellow colour of the yolk.

If this is to hold true, then the flesh-eating birds and other egg-laying vertebrates, in particular the fish- and worm-eating ones, would have to possess no yellow egg-yolk. So far as is known to me, however, all have yellow egg-yolks, even if not always of hen's-egg-yolk yellow!

If one finds the supplied xanthophyll precisely in the egg-yolk, like the lutein, then in my opinion this is to be traced back to the fat-solubility of both. Likewise chlorophyll and animal green [Tiergrün] can be found side by side at the same places, owing to their similar solution relations, without one therefore having to think of an origin of the animal green from chlorophyll.

In their investigations into the sexual dimorphism of the lepidopteran blood colour, Steche (1912) and Geyer (1913) have come back to the view of Poulton (1885) and Countess von Linden (1903), that the green colour is to be ascribed to only slightly altered chlorophyll from the leaf-food. Their spectroscopic investigations show, as they themselves concede, the absence of the characteristic chlorophyll band in the green in the case of E, when the green blood had been investigated under the same conditions — ether solution, 20 mm layer thickness — and thus fully confirm this difference between animal- and plant-green which I indicated earlier (Przibram, 1913).¹ According to the same authors, the spectrum found is also identical with none of the breakdown products of chlorophyll hitherto become known, and, according to all our other experiences, the almost unaltered passage of chlorophyll through the gut meets with considerable difficulties. All this confirms me in the view that it is a matter of a build-up of the "animal green" [Tiergrün] as a proper product of the animal out of further broken-down fragments of the supplied nourishment, whose special components here, as little as elsewhere in nutrition, share in race-characters.

In this connection I should like also to supplement my earlier communications on the rearing of green mantids on absolutely chlorophyll-free fare, to the effect that I was able to continue the experiments on *Sphodromantis bioculata* through several generations and to obtain green specimens again and again (likewise proceeded the rearings of Hári in Budapest, to whom I had left over the same rearing- and food-material).

The animals thus certainly themselves form the yellow and green colouring matters, which on account of their fat-solubility we may place in the group of the lipochromes, although with sulphuric acid they by no means always yield that beautiful blue colour which the bonellein of the marine worm *Bonellia viridis* (cf. Przibram, 1913) yields.

> ¹ According to Willstätter 1911, a) 539, 524; b) 546, 530 (cf. Czapek 1913).
