In the water extract the colouring matters have a more suspension-like character, which is connected with their absolute or relative water-insolubility — which chlorophyll probably also shares.

Since, after ammonium-sulphate precipitation and heat-coagulation, they pass over from the dry coagulum, with recovered colour, into ether or alcohol, whereas the water does not give them off to these solvents, this speaks for a certain binding of the particles by the water envelope.

Positive statements about the genesis of the animal greens in the animal body could be made if the fermentative origin of the yellow-green colour could be carried out in such quantities that the ammonium-sulphate-precipitated and heated precipitate could be used for the recovery of the colouring matter in ether- or alcohol-solution. Hitherto it was not possible, as mentioned, to demonstrate the identity of the fermentatively arisen green substance with the animal green. This, as well as the influencing of the green colouring matters by light of different wavelengths, are the chief tasks which we have set ourselves further, in supplementation of our views on the causes of animal colouration. On the light-transitoriness of the animal greens in white light I have already pointed in the earlier publications.

## V. Summary

I. The colour-dress [Farbkleidung] of animals is to be founded upon their chemism. For this purpose preliminary experiments were conducted on extracted liquids.

II. Colour-formation-accelerating enzymes [Fermente], the tyrosinases, from animal or plant extracts, showed themselves sensitive to external factors:

1. Blue rays at first inhibit the efficacy of the tyrosinase in comparison to darkness; yellow rays promote it; on longer action upon the tyrosinase this relation reverses.

2. Slight temperature differences are not decisive; only on a pre-warming of the tyrosinase to 40° does a difference make itself felt in tyrosine samples, in so far as the colour is weakened from violet-black towards red.

3. Basic reaction favours red, acid favours blue-violet attack-colour [Angehfarbe]; the inhibiting action of the blue rays would presumably rest upon an increase of the alkalescence, the promoting action of the yellow rays upon an increase of the acidity. (At minimal tyrosinase action, green attack-colours occur.)

III. The chromogens fermentatively transforming themselves into colouring matters — the tyrosine or other animal extracts — conformed here to the following rules:

1. The colour-formation takes place under uptake of oxygen and under release of carbonic acid [carbon dioxide] and ammonia.

2. Simultaneously present, in the same solution, inhibiting and promoting tyrosinases do not influence one another in their action, so that, with incomplete intermixing, different drops can yield non-attacking and well-attacking samples.

3. The attack-colour [Angehfarbe] is dependent on the tyrosinase used; the chromogen yields a change of the colour only in so far as it had not been kept water-clear from the outset.

IV. In the animal body, in general, three groups of pigments are produced:

1. Black pigments or melanins fall out from their solutions, on weak acid additions and even on neutral-salt addition, in fibrinous-net-like forms.

2. Red pigments arise from tyrosine on warming to 40° or on tyrosinase action, as a lower oxidative intermediate stage to the melanins, and probably analogously in the case of other chromogens.

3. Yellow and green pigments circulate as fat-soluble "lipochromes" in the tissue fluids; their relation to the water-soluble chromogens is not yet incontrovertibly proven. They contain chlorophyll at most as an admixture in plant-eating species, but arise as animal proper-products even in pure flesh-eaters.

**Abbreviations preserved as printed:** "Vm." = vormittags (a.m.); "Nm." = nachmittags (p.m.); "M." = Mittag (midday/noon); "V." = Vormittag (a.m.); "ʰ" = o'clock/hour; "Tr." = Tropfen (drop[s]), rendered "dr."; "konz." = konzentriert (conc.); "norm." = normal (solution); "bzw." = beziehungsweise ("resp."); "eisenviolett" rendered "iron-violet"; "krapprot" rendered "madder-red"; "⊖" = the printed negative/no-reaction symbol; "⊕" = the printed positive/onset symbol; `"` = ditto; "—" = dash as printed. Taxonomic names, dates, measurements and formulae are left exactly as printed (including the printed "Cavia cobaja" and the abbreviation "Agaricus mell." for *Agaricus melleus*).

These pages are entirely **tables** (the experimental appendix), printed across **facing-page spreads**: the verso pages (166/168/170/172) carry the left-hand identification columns, while the recto pages 167/169/171/173 (the owned recto pages) carry the **right-hand continuation columns**. Each continuation table below is reproduced together with the left-hand columns of the same rows (read from the facing verso) so that no cell is omitted, with each source page anchored by its `` comment.

Right-hand (continuation) columns of the tyrosinase table (rows Or.-Nr. 243–257), headed:

| Datum der Aufstellung [Date of set-up] | Daten der Reihenfolge des Angehens: Farbtöne der Proben (bei Or.-Nr. 198–212 sind diese Farbtöne eingeklammert und die nichteingeklammerten bedeuten dann Farben der Kästchen) [Data on the sequence of onset: colour-tones of the samples (for Or.-No. 198–212 these colour-tones are placed in parentheses, and the non-parenthesized ones then signify colours of the little boxes)] |
|---|---|
| 23. III. 17 | gelblich [yellowish]. |
| » | rötlichlila [reddish-lilac]; 24. II. Vm. 9½ʰ: wasserhell [water-clear]. |
| 24. II. 17 | 26. II. Vm.: violett [violet]. |
| » | 26. II. Vm.: grau, sinkend [grey, sinking]; 28. II.: violettgrau [violet-grey]. |
| » | 26. II. Vm.: grau, steigend [grey, rising]; 28. II.: violettgrau [violet-grey]. |
| » | 26. II. Vm.: silbergrau, diffus [silver-grey, diffuse]; 28. II.: gelbgrau [yellow-grey]. |

## 1. Melanins.

The colour designations denote colour modifications of the living *Dixippus* (1–3 = increasing degrees of darkness); the arrangement of these [or the letter a–f] denotes the sequence of melanin formation, from the weakest to the strongest blackening, in the test tubes charged with the corresponding tyrosinases, each with 1 cm³ of tyrosine [or, in square brackets, ½ cm³ each of the chromogens of the modifications]:

braun 1, braun 3, grün 2, grün 1, braun 2, schwarz, rot.
», grün 2, braun 3, », », », »
braun 2, braun 1, braun 3, grün 2, grün 1, —, —
—, —, —, — grün 2 —, —, —
braun 2, braun 1, braun 3, grün 2, rot, schwarz, grün 1.

15.–19. I. all samples except braun 1 and braun 2, which scarcely took on colour, and grün 1, which was violet, [turned] greenish.

The following matrix (preserving the printed cell layout). Header row: "Tyrosinasen:" (chromogens across the columns); the rightmost column is "Figures above the melanin formation, 17. I. evening [identical within each horizontal row]":

```
Chromogen | br.1 | br.2 | br.3 | gr.1 | gr.2 | rot  | schw. | [identical within each horizontal row]
braun 1   |  a   |      |  c   |  b   |      |      |       | finely radiate, bluish breath
  »   2    |      |  c   |  b   |  a   |      |      |       | no figure
  »   3    |  a   |      |  b   |  c   |  d   | e == f |     | club-shaped, white wreath
grün 1    |      |  c   | b? == a |   |      |      |  d    | needle-shaped,  »   »
  »   2    |      |      |  b?  |  a   |  c?  |  d   |       |    »   bluish   »
rot       |      |      |  b?  |  a   |  c?  |  d   |  e    | dotted, white wreath
schwarz]  |      |      | b? == a |   |      |      |  c    | speckled,  »   »
```

## Table F. Initial colours and colour-changes of the tyrosine tests.

This is one large table with rows **a)–n)** (the *Agaricus* tyrosinase dissolved in the listed reagents) and column groups **I–XIV** spread across four source-page spreads. Within each cell an Or.-No. and a colour description are given. The table is reproduced here group by group, all keyed to the same rows a)–n). "⊕" reproduces the crossed-circle symbol printed in the original.

Column-group headings (columns I–III, this page):
- **I.** Or.-No. — Dissolved in the preceding reagents
- **II.** Or.-No. — In NaOH 0.05%, of the preceding reagent, subsequently filtered
- **III.** Or.-No. — Filter-residue dissolved in H₂O and treated with the preceding reagent

## VII. Bibliography.

**Adler, Oskar**, *Formation of melanotic pigments in sunlight.* Proceedings of the Scientific Society of German Physicians in Bohemia in the year 1913. 46. Prague. Published by the author. 1914.

**Baumann, E.**, *On the determination of homogentisic acid in alkaptonuric urine.* Zeitschr. f. physiologische Chemie. XVI. 268. 1891.

**Bertel, R.**, *On tyrosine breakdown in germinating plants.* Berichte der deutschen botanischen Gesellschaft. XX. 45. 1902.

**Bertrand, G.**, *On a new oxidase, or soluble oxidizing ferment, of vegetable origin.* Comptes rendus académie Paris. CXXII. 1215. 1896 (I).

— *On the simultaneous presence of laccase and tyrosinase in the juice of certain fungi.* Comptes rendus Ac. Paris. CXXIII. 463. 1896 (II).

**Biedermann, W.**, *Colour and marking of insects.* Winterstein's Handbuch der vergleichenden Physiologie. Jena. Fischer. III. 1st half. 1657. 1914.

**Bloch, B.**, *Chemical investigations on the specific pigment-forming ferment of the skin, the Dopa-oxidase.* Zeitschr. f. physiol. Chemie. XCVIII. 226. 1916.

**Bourquelot, Em.**, *Influence of the reaction of the medium on the activity of the oxidizing ferment of fungi.* Comptes rendus Ac. Paris. CXXIII. 260. 1896 (II).

**Brecher, Leonore**, *The pupal colourations of the cabbage white. Pieris brassicae L. Parts I–III.* Akademischer Anzeiger Wien. No. 16. 1916.

— *The pupal colourations of the cabbage white. Pieris brassicae L. Parts I–III.* Archiv für Entwicklungsmechanik. XLIII. 88. 1917.

**Cramer, W. and Bearn, A. R.**, *The effect of heat on enzyme activity.* Journal of Physiology. XXXIV. (p. XXXVI; Proceedings Physiological Society.) 1906.

**Czapek, F.**, *Metabolic processes in the geotropically stimulated root tip and in phototropically sensitive organs.* Berichte der botanischen Gesellschaft. XX. 464. 1902.

— *Antiferments in the plant organism.* Berichte der botanischen Gesellschaft. XXI. 229. 1903.

— *Metabolic processes under hydrotropic and under phototropic stimulation.* Berichte der botanischen Gesellschaft. XXI. 243. 1903.

— *Biochemie der Pflanzen. 2nd edition. Volume I.* Jena. Fischer. 1913.

**Dewitz, J.**, *Investigations on the transformation of insect larvae.* Arch. f. Anatomie und Physiologie; physiol. Abteilung. 327. 1902.

— *The hydrogen-peroxide-decomposing capacity of the male and female butterfly pupae.* Zentralbl. f. Physiologie. XXII. 145. 1908.

— *Investigations with the blood fluid (haemolymph) of insects.* Zentralblatt f. Physiologie. XXXVI. 215. 1912.

— *Investigations on sex differences. No. 4, The behaviour of the two sexes toward methylene blue.* Zoologische Jahrbücher, Abt. f. allg. Zool. u. Physiol. XXXVI. 11. 1916.

**Diesselhorst, H., and Freundlich, H.**, *Fibrin as an anisotropic, amorphous-solid substance.* Internationale Zeitschr. f. physik. chem. Biologie. III. 46. 1916.

**Dürken, Bernhard**, *On the effect of differently coloured surroundings on the variation of butterfly pupae. Experiments on Pieris brassicae.* Zeitschr. f. wissenschaftliche Zoologie. CXVI. 587. 1916.

**Ewald, Walter**, *The physiology of the oxidative blood ferments.* Pflügers Archiv. CXVI. 334. 1907.

**Fuchs, R. F.**, *Colour change and the chromatic skin function of animals.* Wintersteins Handbuch der vergleichenden Physiologie. III. 1st half. 1189. 1913–14.

**Fürth, Otto v.**, *On the action of nitric acid on protein substances.* Habilitation thesis. Straßburg, Buchdruckerei Goeller. 1899.

— *Physiological and chemical investigations on melanotic pigments.* Zentralblatt f. allgemeine Pathologie und pathologische Anatomie. XV. 617. 1904.

— *and Jerusalem, Ernst*, *On the knowledge of melanotic pigments and of fermentative melanin formation.* Hofmeisters Beiträge zur chemischen Physiologie und Pathologie. X. 131. 1907.

— *and Schneider, H.*, *On animal tyrosinases and their relations to pigment formation.* Hofmeisters Beiträge. I. 229. 1901.

**Gessard, C.**, *Studies on tyrosinase.* Annales de l'institut Pasteur. XV. 593. 1901.

**Gonnermann, M.**, *Homogentisic acid, the colour-determining substance of dark beet juices.* Pflügers Archiv. LXXXII. 289. 1900.

**Gortner, R. A.**, *Melanin I. The pigmentation of the mealworm.* Journal of Biological chemistry. VII. 365. 1910. — *II. The pigmentation of the adult periodical cicada.* X. 89. 1911. — *III. The inhibitory action of certain phenolic substances upon Tyrosinase.* X. 113. 1911.
